FRESH FOOD & ICE CO., U°
Fish ! Fish ! Fish !
(AWING to perfect arrangements in the FISH and GAME
DEPARTMENT, the Company is now supplying FISH
to an enormous section of the inhabitants of tlie city, and
residents in every country town of N.S.W.—as a matter
of fact, to many towns outside, notably Brisbane.
A telegraph order from any country town despatched
before 1 p.m. (provided the Telegraph Department do
their part) is certain of execution by mail train same day,
and as Pish is carried at half parcels rates- equal to about
Jd. per lb. for the most distant towns—the luxury of a
fish diet is within the reach of all, the F.P. & I. Co.’s
prices (free on rail) being extremely moderate. We pre-
pay trainage, thereby effecting a saving of 50 percent.
The Company stock all kinds of New Zealand and N.S.
Wales fish, also some American kinds—notably Salmon—
and arrangements have been concluded with the Orient
Line to fit a steamer. The first consignment of English
fish and game leaves London sth May, 1893.
Price-lists obtainable at Company’s Depot, 135 King-st.,
or from the Manager, 25 to 33 Harbour-street.
Note. - We shall be pleased for country visitors to in-
spect o : r refrigerating establishment at. Harbour-street.
The Company deliver daily to all parts of the city and
suburbs- Milk, Ice, Cream, Butter, Poultry, Fish, Game,
Pastry, &c.
Hy. Fatesou, Manager.

This eminent Specialist, Founder, and Chief

known Dispensary that now bears his name, re-
ceived his medical education and surgical training
in the leading colleges and hospitals of Europe,
lie has devoted a life- time to the study of
Nervous Complaints.
He has by profound research thoroughly investi-

cated; with these widely recognised qualifications
combined with unswerving faithfulness to his

over FORTY YEARS, attained a well-deserved
pre-eminence in their treatment. His practice
embraces diseases which probably cause more un-
happiness than any other class of troubles that
afflict mankind.

from headaches, drowsiness during the day, circles

Brushwood, Butter-bush, and ALL NOXIOUS
PLANTS, are quickly and effectually destroyed,
whilst, strange to say,
CLOVER GRASS
IS PRODUCED INSTEAD.
This has been incontestably proved by crucial
tests experimentally made by the Agricultural
Department of New South Wales at Richmond,
under the supervision of J. L. Thompson, Esq.,
in the presence of the well-known botanists, Mr.
Turner and Mr. Ednie Browne (Director-General
of Forests), and other skilled scientific agricultural
experts.

THE
NAMBROK ESTATE,
situated on the Latrobe River, Gippsland,
comprises 15,000 acres of very rich land,
which can scarcely be surpassed for agri-
cultural, fattening and dairy purposes. The
land is specially adapted for the growth of
maize, bops, root crops and cereals, and is
practically ready for the plough. The
Nambrok sheep and cattle have been for
many years noted for the excellence and
high prices obtained. The rainfall is abun-
dant, averaging about 30 inches per annum,
and the climate salubrious, being free from
extreme heat and cold. An unlimited
supply of water can be obtained at a mode-
rate depth, under 30 feet, in any portion of
the Estate.
The Thompson River, which is close to
the northern boundary of the property, can
be easily and cheaply utilised for irrigation
purposes. The Rosedale Railway Station,
111 miles from Melbourne, is within
miles of the property, and the railway line
runs directly through the property, and
the Heyfield station on the Maffra line is
about 5 miles distant.
THE
MELTON PARK ESTATE
comprises 7146 acres, close to Bacchus
Marsh, in the Parish of Yancardook,
County of Bourke, and is well known in l
Victoria for its great fertility and resi-
dential attractions. It is about a couple of
miles from the Melton Railway Station;
20 miles distant from'Melbourne, where an'
ever ready market exists for everything
the Estate produces;
Plans of both Estates will bs forwarded 1
on application.

Orange Blossoms or Weeds.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
They had been engaged for three years;
The end for which the universe had striven ever
I since the Deluge—namely, the union of their two
iexistences,- was not yet accomplished. _ It still
needed all 1 the ingenuity of which his bank-
cler'kish' mind was capable, and all the persuasive-
ness of which she Was possessed to overcome the
i opposition of innumerable relations on both sides,
i The engagement was sanctioned That was
I very little. It was like stamping- a letter and for-
, getting to post it. „
He was in receipt of £l5O a year. Many are
'married and miserable on less than that. It now
' crossed his mind that they might as well follow
I their parents’ example and venture on clandestine
marriage. , , .
Bessie was the eldest daughter of a Mayor ot
on-e of Sydney’s suburbs, and she wanted a real
i wedding ’’—-slippers and rice, and cake and orange
blossoms—also, a few presents, and a newspaper-
paragraph. She was very firm. Lifting her
sweet brown eyes to his pleading face, she_ said
“ No, George, not even for you will I deceive my
father and mother. If you love me don t ask it
of IHG#**
“ Vou do not love me,” said Georgeif you
did, you would let the whole world go and cling to
me. Bessie, my darling— Bessie—look at me.
Tell me you will let them all go, and come tome.
But Bessie was as adamant. It was not tor
nothing that she had a Mayor for a father; some
of his blood was in her veins. When she saw how
duty’s finger pointed, she trampled even her anec-
t'ions under foot, and trod that way.
George was in’ despair. Unspeakable tear
tugged at his heart, while Bessie strained at a
came to the conclusion that he did not know
where he would be in another year or two. Also,
he did not know where Bessie would be. l hat
last thought was terrible ; it brought the perspira-
tion to his brow. ,
One by one, the days came and went; he could
not have endured more than one at a time ; once
he could have taken seven all together. I hat was
when he saw Bessie every evening ; now they met
but once a week. , , -
He was quite calm when next he sought ner,
but misery was written in every line of his race,
in every look of the eyes she had learned to love
“ Don’t touch me, Bessie !” he said, holding her
in his arms and kissing her, “ Help me to be
strong—to do what is right. I want your help,
She crept closer and laid one trembling hand on
his shoulder: “ You’rein some awful trouble, dear,
she said. , T .
George groaned. “ I love you soi and I mus
give you up,” he cried. „„
“ Give me up ! Give me up and you love me.
“God help me, yes. Bessie, little love, be
brave. You do love me, don fc you ?
Her look was more convincing than volumes oi
affirmatives. . -
“Bessie, I’m ill—very ill—con-confoundedly
ill.” That was breaking the news. As she was
only regarding him with dilating eyes, and still
breathing, he added, “ I’m worse than I can quite
grasp yet. Little love, for all our years of wait-
ing, it is no use —I’m a dying got
heart-disease, and nothing can save me.
Such an ending to their courtship they had
never conceived. Words failed the girl; she could
Only grasp him with both hands, as though a
pawning grave were around them. Suddenly he
caught het closet to him.
“ I’ve one year,” he whispered, one little year.
Ob, don’t refuse me. 1 came to give y Oll U P a .
cannot- 1 love you. Ilove.yod. Oh, my daring,
let my last year be spent with you. From all the
years of my life give me one—one year—one
twelve months. Think how I love you, think how
you love me. Think how true you are.
After all, Bessie was first a lover and then a
Mayor’s daughter. She forgot all her dreams, and
her parents, and the world. She remembered only
( ' Omfday she signed away the name the Mayor
nad given her, and took one from a bank-clerk,
with only an old woman and a deaf clergyman in
the church—and no orange blossoms at all.
The days continued to drop away one after the
other just as usual—perhaps they went a little
quicker. And every day fought George one day
nearer to the grave. Bessie would wake up in the
night with the thought gnawing at her heart, it
was there always ; it never left her. M
George and she went into lodgings. The Mayor
and Mayoress and several of their children cut
them. Poverty looked in at every pane of the
window as well as from the door. Three months
slipped away, and after those another three.
Damocles’ sword was suspended in monthly
notches. Only six more slips and the last notch
would be past, and it would be upon them.
The symptoms were developing. George grew
pale and thin. Bessie said it was worry. Helosfc
his appetite, and she blamed the bank. Then he
became irritable and acquired an extra pain m his
left side. To that she could say nothing—there
was nothing to say or do. Only watch and pray
—and endure.
And the sword slipped a few more notches.
Bessie needed all her strength now merely to hve.
George was irritable, overbearing, selfish, dog-
matic. She meekly bowed her head to it all. it
was a consequence of his disease, and what wife
would not endure martyrdom with widowhood
only a notch or two distant ? , .
And the sword slipped three more notches. Any
day it might fall now. Bessie grew irritable
under her great suspense. She contradicted
George twice, she stamped at him three times,
and once she slammed the door in lus face. I hen
repentance overtook her, and she wept.
The days grew into months, and the months
into years, and George yet lived. The sword,
like Bessie’s forbearance, was gone. They forgot
there was such a thing as death-except for
others
Bessie has lost her orange-blossoms—if one can
lose what one never had—and now she be #’ ns ,
fear she will lose what she will never have-her
weeds. George is still pale and irritable, and his
disease is now fully developed-indigestion.
Bessie is embittered. She can only hope her
reward may be in Heaven, but she has been taught
that weeds are not from there.
Sydney.
LILIAN J. TURNER.
“No one wishes to be unjust to tee-
totallers and moralists,” says G, R. Sims; “but
it is a remarkable fact that most of the gigantic
commercial frauds of recent times have been asso-
ciated with the names of gentlemen belonging to
the ancient order of Pharisees. The cloak of the
Puritan too frequently hides the shirt of Lai tune,
and the swindling bank-director sits down to pre-
pare a bogus -balance-sheet after presenting a
thousand pounds of somebody else’s money to a
fund for the enlargement of the local chapel.
Yet Sims had his eye upon England and not on
Australia when he wrote that.

NOTICE.
By sending direct to Tub Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings sterling—with postage added where required,
which you may pay by Post Office Order, One Pound
Note, or stamps of any Australian province—you can
have Tub Bulletin posted regularly to you every week for
a year.
This offer is not open to residents of Sydney. In that
city The Bulletin is not delivered by mail, but is entirely
in the hands of our carriers, with whom we do not wish
to interfere.
Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communications
to be addressed to William Macleod, Managing Director.
Literary Communications to be addressed J. F.
Archibald.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
*Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted—
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing ivith Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or say 3000 laords, are specially acceptable).
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
Tub Bulletin will return all unavailable MSS
when the address is specified and stamps are enclosed.
But we desire those sending MSS. to distinctly under-
stand that we are not responsible for the preservation or
transmission of such MSS.

SATURDAY. MAY 27, 1893.
Wanted—A State Audit Department.
The amazing collapse, in less than fourteen
months, of fourteen banks out of the twenty-
two Avhich, at the beginning of last year, were
trading in Australia, is still another evidence
of the urgent necessity for a State Audit
Department in these provinces. The same
lesson Avas taught before in the fall of the
Van Dieman’s Land Bank, the Commercial
of South Australia, the Real Estate, Stan-
dard, Imperial, and Anglo-Australian, the
famous, or infamous, Premier Permanent
Society, and a hundred other like disasters,
but it was never so strongly impressed upon
the nation as by the universal smash of the
past few Aveeks, And, so far as the available
evidence goes, there is absolutely no
guarantee that the collapse of the next few
weeks may not furnish a still more definite
proof of the necessity of displacing Ananias,
the auditor, Avith some independent public
authority ; for whether this country is at the
end, or the middle, or only at the beginning
of its financial troubles no one can tell. The
one thing that can be told Avith certainty is
that all—or nearly all—directors are liars,
and that there is no faith in auditors, and
that no balance-sheet Avhich is compounded
by any son of man, except under absolutely
independent and even hostile supervision, is
of any account whatsoever.
# # •
The private auditing farce is played out.
The auditor, in the vast majority of cases, is
practically the appointee and the bond-slave
of the directors, and his livelihood depends
upon his readiness to swear, at all times and
seasons, that everything is right, whether it
is right or not. The business of an auditor
is to be blind in one eye, and to see nothing
that he is not desired to see Avith the other ;
and the man who does not fill these require-
ments falls into ill-odour and loses his
employment. The remedy which the share-
holders or other persons interested have
against either the directors or auditors is
practically nil. The average board is a
species of secret society or irremoveable
oligarchy. It can only be shifted by a
majority vote at a meeting of shareholders,
and by gathering up its friends and rela-
tions, and those members of the company
who can be influenced by overdrafts and
other devices, and scooping in proxies
from indifferent, ignorant, and helpless
people at a distance, it can almost always
vote down a recalcitrant faction if it is
worth its while to do so. Moreover,
even if it is possible to shift a board, or a
section thereof, Avhich is suspected of cor-
ruption or incapacity, it can only, in the case
of a large institution with a widespread pro-
prietary, be done by an agitation and public
scandal and general upheaval which adver-
tises the alleged rottenness of its finances to
the furthest ends of the earth. In the case
of a bank—Avhich depends so largely on the
estimation in which it is held by the public—
such a proceeding means probably the start-
ing of a panic which may end in ruin ; in the
case of a life-insurance office it means a
malodorous advertisement which may destroy
the progress of a decade. And even if the
change is accomplished, there is absolutely
no more effectual check on the new board
than there was on the old one. Therefore
the directors and the auditors generally
“ fake” their deceptive sheet without hind-
rance until the crash comes and proves, for
the hundredth time, that the accounts might
as profitably have been inspected by a
chimpanzee.
* • *
The necessity for a State Audit Depart-
ment is emphasised by the utter meaning-
lessness of the average balance-sheet. The
significance of every bank’s statement of
accounts lies in the item “ all other
advances,” which generally forms three-
fourths of the assets, and ranges anywhere
from tens of thousands to tens of millions,
according to the size of the institution.
Whether these advances are fully secured,
or Avhether the security has depreciated five
or 100 per cent., or Avhether a great part of
them never had any tangible security at all,
the average balance-sheet does not eA’en
pretend to say. The same criticism applies,
in a less degree, to the “mortgages”
in the annual statement of every life-assur-
ance office, “land-bank,” and building
society. It is impossible for those interested
to ascertain the value or valuelessness of the
alleged asset except by virtually deposing the
directors and auditors, and insisting on Avhat
is practically a hostile investigation, and it is
impossible to get such an investigation until
the suspicions of a large number of the share-
holders are seriously roused, and owing to
the board’s special facilities for concealment
and perversion of the truth such sus-
picions hardly ever are roused until the
■uin is past remedy, and seldom even
then. Moreover, such an investigation, as
already pointed out, is practically an an-
nouncement to the whole world of presum-
able insolvency. What is wanted is not the
spasmodic kind of independent audit which
never, by any chance, comes until it is too
late, but a continuous inspection by an abso-
lutely independent authority, Avhich will do
its work Avhether suspicion is aroused or not,
Avhich Avill be superior to bribes or extraneous
influences, which will regard managers and
auditors and boards of directors Avith the
same hostile eye that the policeman casts
on the seedy outcast in the park, and
which will deal out the same measure to the
biggest bank and the biggest insurance
society as it does to the humblest building
society in a back-blocks township. Such an
institution would form the ideal Audit
Department, and the ideal head of that de-
partment Avould be an icy mathematician of
the unbending suspicion and practicalness of
the experienced Hebrew usurer, a man
devoted Avholly to figures, an orphan, friend-
less, and a celibate, a deformed and boAV-
legged individual whose hideousness Avould
put him beyond the range of “society”
influence, a teetotaller and a misanthrope.
If such an individual could be found and
hired at £lO,OOO per annum to be the enemy
of the board of every financial institution
which takes in deposits or premiums or
otherwise deals with the money and con-
fidence of the public, the investment would
be the very best which the State has ever yet
made.
• • •
The scope for a public officer of this de-
scription is enormous. Scores and scores of
the benefit and friendly societies of Aus-
tralasia are insolvent from an actuary’s
standpoint, or else on the brink of insol-
vency. They nearly all offer benefits which
are more or less out of keeping with the pre-
miums levied on their members, and exist
mostly — as has just been slioavii by N.S. W.
State statistician Coghlan with respect to
some of their number—by trusting to Provi-
dence and passing on the responsibility to the
morrow. Somewhere over one-half of the
building societies in this country have re-
cently suspended, or “reconstructed,” or
gone utterly and hopelessly bankrupt with
next to nothing in the £, and over many
more hangs the sword. All these disastrous
institutions, from the Premier Permanent
downwards, were sworn to as solvent by
their directors and auditors to the bitter
end. Nine-tenths of the “land-banks”
have gone more or less horribly, and every
one of them also Avas audited and proved to
be in a state of glorious prosperity almost
to the last day of its miserable existence.
Two-thirds of the banks of issue have
collapsed, after declaring big dividends and
carrying large alleged sums to the reserve-
fund amid general enthusiasm. And as all
these banks, except the Federal and the
malodorous Mercantile, have been, or are
being, “ reconstructed ” by the same crowd
which SAvore to their solvency until they
hurst, and as the mysterious item, “ other
advances, £10,000,000,” or Avhatever the
amount may be, remains as inexplicable as
ever, the need for State audit is, in their
case, as great now as it was before. In most
instances the only inspection made has been
by the individuals whose incapacity, or per-
jury, or dishonesty, or whatever it might be,
was so fully demonstrated by the original
suspension, and therefore, for all the evi-
dence to the contrary, the re-organised in-
stitution is almost as legitimate an object of
suspicion as Davies’ famous speculation,
whose misfortune it was that it died before
re-organisation Avas invented.
# • »
Furthermore, the same suspicion extends
to at least some of the institutions which
have survived the panic—in fact, it ex-
tends everyAvbere. A sound institution
which rashly locks up too much of its
money in advances may succumb to a
sudden run ; a hopelessly rotten and fraudu-
lent one which turns everything into cash
and whose alleged “ advances” and “securi-
ties ” are absolutely non-existent, may out-
live the run, and keep going from bad to worse
and gathering in deposits for many years.
In some cases, indeed, the most insolvent
bank has the best chance at a crisis, for the
mere knowledge that suspension may mean
inspection, and inspection must mean penal
servitude, causes the board and Ananias,
the auditor, to prepare more diligently for
emergencies, and gather in fixed deposits
more ardently that they may pay immediate
liabilities out of the proceeds. And —though
in a less degree —the same necessity for
State supervision exists in the case of life-
insurance societies. The advantage of a dis-
honest life insurance society lies in the fact
that it cannot be rushed by its clients after
the manner of a bank, and so long as the
premiums come hi freely it can disguise its
condition almost indefinitely. The further
advantage of all insurance societies, honest
or otherwise, lies in the fact that—not being
subject to “ runs ” —they can “ nurse ” their
bad investments, if necessary, for genera-
tions, and a very large percentage of bad
investments turn out good if only the
investor can Avait till the boom returns, as it
always does return sooner or later. Never-
theless, the fall of one of the great life-
offices of Australia would mean almost as much
ultimate disaster to the poor as the collapse of
half-a-dozen banks, and the want of efficient
State supervision in their case is a horrible
example of the insincerity of Australian
legislation and legislators.
The “Private Enterprise” Boom.
Success has so far attended the efforts of
MTlvvraith, and those Avho stand behind
him, to straddle, possess, and devour
Queensland. From present appearances,
nothing short of a miracle can save the
province from strangulation. The best that
can be hoped for in the present Parliament
is the persistent barking of a few watch-dogs
at a gang of political looters Whom they can-
not bite. Even some of those may be
nobbled or muzzled by the great artist who
has engineered affairs to the present posi-
tion. But, anyhow, the prospect, Avith a
handful of Labour members, the remnant of
an Opposition tbut was no better than it
should be> and a few “independent” rail-
sitters, is a gloomy one for comparatively
honest government. The game lias been
dexterously played and the syndicates have
Avon. Before the electors can, in the ordinary
course of things, speak again, the partition
of the country’s remaining lands Avill pro-
bably have proceeded far. The ligatures that
are to stop the circulation of its life-blood
will be tied on and ready for tightening.
Such raA’enous and parasitical “ vested in-
terests ” Avill have been entrenched in a way
that hardly anything but melinite and
bayonets can shift them.
# # #
And, as we foresaw, the triumph of the
plotters against Queensland has emboldened
those Avho are hungering to raid New South
Wales. The vulturine claw now visibly
steals from out the monopolistic coat-sleeVe.
A feast for kites and vampires has been
spread in Queensland ; the cloth is being
laid in Sydney. The condition into which
public finances Avere alloAved to drift in
Queensland Avas made the excuse for re-
placing State initiative by “ private enter-
prise.” The credit of the province was
worked to death in raising loans, most
of which directly or indirectly enriched per-
sons and corporations, Avhile impoverishing
the people as a Avhole. Then, Avhen the
ordinary sources of revenue for constructing
public works had been destroyed, and a set-
tled frown brought upon the face of Lom-
bard-street, the advantages of doing by
“ private ’’-—mostly very private—“enter-
prise” Avhat could not be done by other
means were dwelt upon. Tloav to find
employment for a heavily-taxed and stone-
broke population was the problem. Public
Avorks were wanted ; public works would give
profitable employment to the nubile. Public
money could not be obtained ; avliv not then
let public-spiiited private persons undertake
these things 1 It Avas a plausible line of
argument. Once get the stomachs of yonr
listeners empty, and any argument that pro-
mises to fill them, even temporarily, sounds
plausible. In Queensland some voters wore
in the swim, and these had most power.
Others, a few, resolutely ojiposed the
whole infamous business. But the vote
Avhich has given Queensland over to
the spoilers was that of those Avhose
poverty, but not their will, consented. Of
the supporters of the land-grant railway aUcl
the Kanaka, probably a big majority hated
both. To secure present employment in the
feverish boom of railway-building, or in
supplying slaves, they have sold their Owh
children into slavery.
* # *
Practically, the same conditions prevail in
New South Wales. For long past the
Government has been unable to proceed
with large national works. For long to
come it will probably be unable to pay
current expenses, unless the Fat Man is
tapped, which contingency yet seems afar
off. The unemployed increase, wages are
cut down, retrenchment all round is adopted,
business falls off’, depression deepens. The
public ear inclines to any scheme that will
bring relief, and bring it quickly. That
is the time to pour into it stories of the
prosperity that will result if “ private enter-
prise ” is allowed a free hand in a large and
generous way. Within the last fortnight
there has been a remarkable unanimity of
opinion in the Sydney press that “ private
enterprise ’’ has been monstrously suppressed
in the past, and that if the province is to be
dragged'back from the edge of perdition, the
job must be entrusted to syndicates. The
slightest indication of any extension of
municipal functions is promptly, shamelessly,
and with absolute savagery, jumped upon.
The safety of the public is now clearly seen
to lie in this—that the rendering of services
of every kind to the public must be left to
“private enterprise.” The innate per-
niciousness of an attempt by a municipal
council to supply the rate-payers with gas or
electric-light, is proclaimed from the house-
tops and in the organs of the gang, and
the proposal that it shall run a ferry-boat
for public benefit is seen through at a glance,
and exposed as an attack on the sacred prin-
ciple of private enterprise, which must be
defeated at all cost
* * *
The Australian Star has hailed Minister
Lyjne's declaration that in future the New
South Wales Government is not to be looked
to for the construction of public works as a
message of deliverance. It is so in one
sense. It conveys an intimation that, if
possible, the people are to be delivered over
to the syndicates, gagged and bound as they
have been in Queensland. Nothing is
clearer than that all kinds of wires are being
pulled to establish in New South Wales such
a dynasty of privileged blackmailers as has
been inaugurated there. The beginning
will be much more modest, no doubt. If
possible, a private railway or two with sham
provisions to restrain the syndicates from
fleecing the public, then more of the Same
kind, electric-lighting rights, water-power
rights—all sorts of private rights involving
public wrongs. The Bulletin has Said
before that it would be worth a civil war to
prevent a mile of State railway-lines froin
passing into the control of private hands.
And it would be better to go without UeeA-
ful new works to the end of our days thah
have them built oh such terms as would be
asked by and conceded to private syndicated.
* h *
But there is no need to go without theffi.
There is a very simple way of getting them
built without imposing hew taxation on the
workers, and Avithout immolating the people
at the shrine of “ private enterprise.’*
There never was a public undertaking of ally
importance carried out which did not add a
value to the country’s lands equal to the cost of
its construction. What is the matter with a
betterment tax Avhich would take that value
to recoup the expenditure ? That is how the
London County Council is going to proceed.
The members of that body, and of the
Glasgow and Manchester County Councils,
do not propose abandoning public AvOfkS
because they have no funds. They propose
compelling those Avho Will be specially behe-
fitted to become responsible for their share
of the benefit. Instead of trusting to private
enterprise, they are extending the sphere of
municipal activities, and tendering public
service in many directions better and more
cheaply than it Avas ever rendered before.
And it is to such examples, and not to that
of Queensland, that Australia must look.
When the Panic is Over.
WHEN the financial panic is over, and all the
banks which have collapsed, or are going to
collapse, have successfully re-constructed, the
people of Australia may possibly find time to
consider what is likely to happen next. So far,
they apparently have not considered this point;
it seems to be generally taken for granted
that the country will be near the end of its
present troubles when the suspended institu-
tions are floated once more on a “ sound basis,
whereas all the signs are that the real disaster
will only then be beginning. The prominent
feature of the re-constructions is the frantic
desire to build up huge capitals—to start each
bank on a more bloated scale than its rivals ;
and when these unnaturally swollen and
dropsical enterprises get into working order
some surprising results must ensue. The in-
crease of what is euphoniously termed “bank-
ing facilities” will be very great, but Aus-
tralia, in its present condition, has no earthly
use for half, or anywhere near half, the said
facilities. And as the banks will have to earn
dividends on three or four times their former
capital they will necessarily be more merciless
usurers than they ever were before, and will
grope more industriously in order to scrape up
the necessary percentage. The Commercial of
Australia alone, when it gets into full blast,
must realise £(500,000 profit annually in order
to pay 10 per cent, to its shareholders. In one
way and another the banking capital of the
country, including “inscribed deposits” and


interminable debentures, which are simply
capital under another name, will probably be
raised from about seventeen millions to some-
thing approaching a hundred, (Hid this awful
xnonument of “ private enterprise ” will entail
a vast burden on the community which keeps
the dividends going.
# v #
Everything included, something approaching
three-fourths of the trading capital of the com-
munity has, during the past two mouths, been
violently seized upon and locked up —much of
it confiscated for ever in the shape of perpetual
deposits, preference-shares, and similar devices,
the rest for various terms, most of them from
seven to fifteen years. In Brisbane, Sydney
and Melbourne, nearly three-fourths of the
traders, shopkeepers, and employers of labour
generally, are thus deprived of their liquid
resources. For the present, the results of this
monetary upheaval have hardly begun to be
apparent. The great majority of small traders
are living for the present by staving off their
creditors, renewing their bills, and obtaining
extensions of credit in various ways ; and the
wholesale dealers who grant these extensions
are doing likewise with their creditors, and so
on all round the circle. Everybody is waiting
till his particular bank is “ re-constructed,” in
the hope that he may be able to sell his pre-
ference shares or debentures or deposit receipts
or other paper currency, and keep afloat with the
proceeds. But, for sufficiently obvious reasons,
bardlv anyone will be able to realise these miser-
able apologies for value received, and when
this fact is generally understood a widespread
insolvency must result. Every one who is
involved in the disaster will begin to sell tip his
debtors in order that he may avoid being him-
self sold up by his creditors, and while he is
doing it he will probably be also sold up by
other men who are actuated by the same
motives. The commercial panic has not yet
begun, but when it begins its end is past fore-
seeing.
# # *
The reason why nobody, or hardly anybody,
will be able to realise his alleged securities
hardly requires explanation. In the first place,
almost everybody will be selling, and as almost
everybody will possess a superfluity of this
kind of paper hardly anybody will be a buyer.
The prospect of a rush setting in for bank
paper is about as remote as the prospect of an
active demand for Argentine securities at
Buenos Ayres. The country will be choke-
full of undigested paper. It will be, in a
certain sense, a forced and irredeemable cur-
rency—shares, debentures, bonds, and deposit-
receipts all alike ; with one added disadvantage
—it will be irredeemable, but it will not be
currency. And buyers will be practically
non-existent for the further reason that the
banks have confiscated the coin with which
alone purchases can be made. Tim capitalist
who is willing to buy up bank paper will, in
many cases, have nothing but similar paper to
give in exchange, and money for speculative
purposes will be almost unobtainable. The
immediate result of the unscrupulous “ water-
ing ” which has taken place will be wholesale
depreciation, and if the process goes much
further the time may yet arrive when bank
shares will be sold by weight, and irredeemable
debentures be traded off by the barrow-load.
* # #
Another inevitable consequence is that the
banks will own Australia, body, soul, and
estate, for many years to come. They have, by
a bushranging process of finance, annexed most
of the trading capital of the country, and fixed
it in their bloated pawnbroking emporiums
either for ever or for a long term of years, and,
having done this, they now promise to alleviate
the disaster by granting overdrafts to the more
solvent of their plundered customers —at the cus-
tomary 8 or 9 per shent, and with such security
as they may think sufficient. Therefore, the
situation of the average trader will be some-
thing like this : He has, say, £lOOO on current
account in a reconstructed bank, which sum is
now transposed into one or other of the various
forms of unsaleable paper at, probably, per
cent, interest. The money being absolutely
necessary to keep his business afloat, he gets an
overdraft—if he is fortunate enough to obtain
one~at 8 per cent., and thus, instead of his
money bringing in a return, he pays £35 per
annum for the use of his own capital. His
debentures, or whatever the printed evidence
of the robbery perpetrated upon him may be
called, being at best worth, probably, only
somewhere about £5OO in the open market, he
has to give security over his property for the
use of his own money. His business goes to
wreck, because the purchasing power of the
community is reduced to zero through the con-
fiscation of capital by the banks, therefore the
hank ultimately sells him up for failure to repay
his own money, and the interest due to the
fellow who stole it, when called upon. And, to
complete his ruin, his property sells for less
than half its value, simply by reason of the fact
that the money of possible purchasers has
already been annexed by one or other of the
hanks, and there is practically no cash avail-
able for speculative purposes. Under such
conditions, the banks will presently have a lien
upon the whole continent of Australia. They
Required a temporary hold upon one-half of it
hy the old-time process, conceived when bank-
lug was still in its infancy, of lending out their
deposits upon mortgage and afterwards fore-
closing. Then, by repudiating their liabilities
ail( l thus making the borrowed money their
cwu, they secured a firm and permanent foot-
hold, and from this vantage-ground they now
reach out for the other half of the country, with
? better prospect than before of not over-
balancing and breaking their necks in the effort.
/ * * *
Taking all things into account, it would pro-
bably have been better for the country if every
jauk which collapsed had been peacefully
hquidated ai) d never again heard of. Australia,
even before the recent panic, had a greater
pressure of bank to the square inch than any
other country on this earth, and now the pres-
',| Ue l ias been trebled. When the “ re-con-
structions ’’are completed, about one-sixth of
he whole wealth of Australia, reckoning in
. 16 continent itself and everything that is upon
I '> will be represented by the paper of these
overgrown pawnshops in all its various shapes.
They will control all the moveable or conveni-
ently realisable wealth in the land. They already
practically own the Legislatures, and with their
increased power and influence they will pre-
sently own them altogether. And there is
absolutely no guarantee against the repetition
of the recent inflating process, with all its
ruinous consequences, whenever the opportunity
arrives. When Australia recovers from its
present difficulties, and partially digests the
great mass of I O U’s which now lie like a
mountain upon its soul, the flow of deposits
will probably begin afresh, and when they
mount up to one or two hundred millions it
will doubtless be found that they also, like the
last lot, can’t be found when wanted, and an
attempt will be made to “ reconstruct ” them
also. Or else, in place of receiving money pay-
able at fixed dates in the old fashion, the
banks will adopt the system of interminable
“ inscribed stock deposits,” which are never
payable at all until the Last Day—and not
even then. And thus the time may come when
Australia will consist entirely of banks—when
the banks will own everything from the Great
Australian Bight to Cape York, and every man
of property will have his w'orldly goods in the
shape of bank shares or debentures, and will
deposit them in the bank for safe keeping. A
very few more “ re-constructions ” like the
present, and the thing would be done. y
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Sydney “Telegraph” on State
Banking.
The report of the committee appointed by the
New South Wales Assembly to consider the
advisability of establishing a State bank, has
roused the Sydney Telegraph to frenzy—a
frenzy of execration. In the course of a column
of print it blasts the committee and all its
works with all the contents of the office dic-
tionary. It curses because the committee
states that “confidence is the foundation of
sound banking ”; the Telegraph has been
saying the same thing for weeks past—at least,
it has been saying, with a horrible iteration, that
the want of confidence is the cause of unsound
banking, which is just about identical—so it
can’t and doesn’t contradict the assertion ; it
merely curses. It curses some more because the
committee remarks that the Government would
inspire more confidence than any private syndi-
cate ; it doesn’t deny this either, but again it
sin:ply curses. Then it professes that the coni-
mitteesuffers from an insane idea “that anational
bank could make stamped paper take the place
of money, and in that way provide all that was
wanted without troubling anybody.” As the
committee suggested the very opposite of this,
and proposed that the .State Bank should issue
its “ stamped paper ” on the same system as is
now adopted, or alleged to be adopted, by the
private banks, this is unscrupulous misrepre-
sentation pure and simple. Also, it badly wants
to know' where the State would get the neces-
sary capital to run the institution. Evidently
it regards this as an absolutely crushing
argument, and the obvious answer —that the
capital, if obtainable in no other way, could
always be got by seizing upon the deposits and
by wholesale repudiation of engagements, after
the fashion now affected by “private enter-
prise,” and that it would be quite as pleasant
for the depositor to be swindled by the State
as by a syndicate—is entirely overlooked.
After this the daily organ proceeds to point out
that the “ forced paper currency ” proposed to
be issued by the State Bank—no such thing is
proposed, but that is a mere side-issue—would
be far more nefarious than that which
now afflicts New South Wales, because the
latter is “issued against ample and obvious secu-
rities.” As it happens, however, the securities
in question, so far as the banks are concerned,
though they may or may not be ample, are
assuredly not obvious, as everybody, including
the Telegraph, is fully aware. Until the
banks are constantly audited by the State,
there will never be anything obvious about
them, and, in any case, an authorised issue of
£7,000,000 in paper against a coin-reserve of
about £2,500,000, which reserve is also the
security for £4,000,000 or more of deposits pay-
able at call, isn’t issuing against “ obvious
securities ” —it is simply a case of relying upon
credit—the same thing which the Telegraph
says the State Bank must not do under pain of
general ruin. But probably the brightest
feature of the article is the Telegraph’s shriek
on the subject of interest :
At present the rate of interest is determined by compe-
tition. Like everything else, the price of money obeys
the law of supply and demand. The banks are obliged,
by the rivalry amongst themselves, to pay the highest
market rate to depositors, and lend to borrowers at the
lowest. It is impossible to see where the cheap money
would come from. If the national bank borrowed it from
depositors they would require the market rate of interest
for it, which would either have to be paid by the customer
or by the taxpayer. According to the committee’s
scheme, the customer would get it below the market
rate, as fixed by unrestricted competition, consequently
the taxpayer would have to make good the difference.
Apart from the facts that the price of money
doesn’t obey the law of supply and demand at
present, but is fixed rather by bulldozing the
depositor, and that the banks have a species of
agreement which entirely prevents “unrestricted
competition,’’the question aboutwhere the cheap
money is to come from is easily answered. The
private banks borrow money at 4 per cent, and
lend it out again at 8 or 10, and pay dividends up
to 25 per cent, thereby. The State can also take
deposits at 4 per cent, and lend them out at 6,
and yet make a good profit on the transaction.
This a'nswers the Telegraph’s great conundrum
in one sentence.
—.—
Village Settlements for the Unemployed.
It is common to dismiss the sorrows of the un-
employed with the remark “ they could obtain
work if they really wanted it.” It pays many
people to relieve their pockets and their con-
sciences of a burden even at the expense of
falsehood. It is not likely that many men
would prefer the joys of an idleness obtained at
the cost of sleeping out in the cold winter
nights, the wearing of filthy rags, and the
racking of dirt-boxes for rejected scraps, to the
enjoyment of even the meanest comforts of un-
remitting toil. Probably one out of every hum
dred of the compulsorily-idle is disinclined to
toil, and it is more than likely that such dis-
inclination arises from some physical or mental
unfitness for which its owner is not personally
responsible. Human beings are not born
lazy. Full of the joy of life, the child’s
greatest pleasure lies in overtaxing its undeve-
loped muscle. Arrived at manhood or woman-
hood, and compelled to labour at uncongenial
tasks during over-lengthy hours, while badly
housed, clothed and fed, and without prospect
of betterment, the day’s work becomes a dis-
tasteful routine, and that physical exhilaration
which finds vent in love of useful exertion is
quickly smothered. Or, the prematurely-
wearied individual is dismissed with the more
diligent because of business depression. All
are deprived of honest opportunities to labour
during weary months, while the faculties
for labour are rusting from disuse. It is
better to wear out than to rust out, says
someone, and Society is lucky if the man
thus misused does not turn out a loafer;
luckier still if he does not become a thief.
Society is willing to accept the respon-
sibilities of its unemployed after they become
loafers or criminals; for them it builds asylums
and gaols, but acts with criminal stupidity
when it ignores their wants and rights prior to
that stage, and thus drives them to swell the
vast army of undesirables. Society, further-
more, acts with contemptible treachery when it
excuses its failure to provide the opportunities
for honest work by stating that they are not
desired. Just now the manufacture of loafer,
prostitute, and criminal is the most flourishing
native industry. Just now the existence of
loafer, prostitute, and criminal is admitted by
the State, while the existence of the disin-
herited, willing to work but unable to find an
opening, is ignored. They will be avenged in
time. Society is its own worst enemy.
The Day of Reckoning.
As the N.S.W. parliamentary session is nearly
over, it might be advisable for some member
to ask the Government —lest the opportunity
should not again present itself—what course it
proposes to adopt when the bank-proclamation
expires. When that event takes place the
Legislature will probably be in recess, and the
Dibbs Ministry will be able to go on its
erratic way without any supervision what-
ever. Under present conditions, the State
has guaranteed the notes of English
and two local banks up to about £7,000,000,
and at least one—almost certainly two—of these
institutions are availing themselves of this
opportunity to send their gold to satisfy certain
rampant creditors in other provinces where
their notes are not blessed with a State endorse-
ment. Only a few days ago about £250,000 (or
semethina like a couple of tons) of gold was
despatched in this manner to Brisbane, and
other shipments are going on at frequent inter-
vals. Therefore, New South Wales, by its
“ vigorous” policy, is supplying gold to all the
other provinces, and practically guaranteeing
the debts of a whole continent. But when the
half-year expires, and a great proportion of
the notes in forced circulation are presented
for payment —what is the outlook? The
limited stock of coin now on hand will have
vanished long before then to pay creditors in
Victoria, Queensland, South Australia, and
elsewhere. The prospect of realising securities
or calling up overdrafts is practically nil; no
securities would realise half their value under
present conditions, and no one can pay his over-
draft, least of all in gold which is the one
essential. Gold is even likely to be somewhat
scarce in the Treasury, for coin is fast vanish-
ing out of circulation, and Nemesis is coming
upon the State in the shape of its own guar-
anteed notes which it will have to present to
itself with a brazen demand for hard money
in exchange. The great expedient of the Dibbs
Cabinet has produced three separate results : it
has postponed the day of reckoning for six
months ; it has shifted the liabilities of the
other provinces on to New South Wales ; and
it has made the State finally responsible for the
lot. And the signs are that, in the end, the
State may have to find £2,000,000 or so to
settle up the liability. It will, without any
possible doubt, find the amount, or five times
as much if needful, but at what a cost ! the
whole business has been supreme foolishness
from first to last. The obvious way out of the
difficulty at the beginning was by an issue of
State notes, to be temporarily declared a legal
tender, which could be advanced to the banks
at a nominal interest on the security of their at
present unrealisable assets. In this way the
Treasury would have been fully protected, the
banks would have been able to obtain loans on
securities which, though intrinsically good, are
utterly useless in the present emergency, and
as the issue could be withdrawn by slow
degrees there would be no sudden and violent
day of reckoning. Bnt this idea might have
led in the end to the establishment of a State
Bank. At all events, it looked too much like
the thin edge of that abhorred possibility, and
for that reason the Dibbs “ private enterprise”
Ministry preferred to guarantee the banks with
no security whatever, and to take the chance of
a run on the emaciated Treasury just when it
will be least able to stand the strain. Ihe State
Bank must come.

The one solid consolation of the “ great ’ daily
papers of Australia, amid the bang and clatter
of falling banks, is that these exploded institu-
tions have brought a lot of British capital into
Australia. Says the Sydney Telegraph on
this subject:—
All the banks which hold large British deposits have
suspended payment, and it is one distinct good out of
things evil that the money remains here.
The Bulletin regretfully confesses that it can’t
see where the “distinct good” comes in. When
a man living, say, at Newtown, goes into Red-
fern and pawns his coat for 205., and brings
the pound back to Newtown, he is bringing
money into his own district in exactly the
same way as the Australian banks have been
bringing it into Australia. And when he can t
get the'eoat out again, and lias to renew the
pawn-ticket for another six months, whereby
the pound remains in the district, lie gets
exactly the same “distinct good as Australia
is now getting nut of the bank suspensions.
And when he keeps on renewing the ticket
until he has paid twice the value of the coat, in
interest, and then gets it out at last and finds it
mostly eaten by moths, he is in precisely the
same blessed situation as this country will be
in when it finally settles up—as it must do
sooner or later. When a paper calls an im-
mense mortgage a “distinct good,” what would it
call a big earthquake or an outbreak of cholera \
Victoria, on the excellent authority of A.N.A.
Purves, is “ overwhelmed by a wave of mis-
fortune, caused by an individual band of
marauders who, in their sinister and evil minds,
conceived schemes of such gross dishonesty that
the fortunes of the people of the province are in
danger. Whether punishment will fall on the
heads of those people he knows not, but it ought
to, because Victoria’s financial credit must be
established in the world before they can step on
to federation.” Having cursed the marauders
in his frenzy of patriotism, Purves proposed to
“ save the country ” on the lines previously laid
down by one Pudney —viz., by a State lottery
distribution of the lands which the aforesaid
marauders are so anxious to get quit of. Pro-
bably the professional instinct of Purves, Q.C.,
came in here. He would prefer, maybe, to have
the marauders supplied with untold gold for
the purpose of briefing fifteen counsel apiece
before the Crown commenced to go for them in
earnest. It is indeed difficult to separate the
native patriot from the barrister in the case of
Purves. Only a few weeks ago, he was
defending, with fiery eloquence, the secretary
of a busted bank that had started with a paid-
up capital of three-and-sixpence, more or less.
Nob a word did Purves say concerning “ gross
dishonesty ” at that time. Certainly his par-
ticular client was bub a small and youthful
marauder, who didn’t know much better.
STevertheless, The Bulletin can’t forget that
the cruelty of the Crown prosecution reduced
poor Purves to absolute tears.
Who, in Victoria, could be safely trusted with
the control of a State Bank? The “ cronk ”
gang would soon have hold of the manager
and directorate (if the manager and direc-
torate didn’t belong to the gang in the first
instance) and they would suck “ temporary
assistance ” from the State Bank for their “ sol-
vent ” banks and building societies until the
country collapsed under its burden of Government
debentures issued to keep the game going. At
the expiration of 10 years of “ inadvisable
clamour” the Slate would make a hollow pre-
tence of prosecuting its own bank. A long
succession of Attorneys-General would carry on
the question of considering whether the evidence
warranted any further proceedings in the
matter, and when it came to the A.G. who was
fairly cornered into saying “Yes,” there would
have to be an adjournment of another 10 years
whilst Theodore Fink was preparing satisfac-
tory evidence for the defence. In the mean-
while Fink would call upon the newspapers to
suppress all ungentlemanly comment, and by the
time everybody concerned was cold and dead, all
necessity for a prosecution would have passed
away. Furthermore, the rats would have eaten
the books of the State Bank during the decades
when the institution was closed by Royal
Proclamation in consequence of the panic.
The suspension of the City of Melbourne Bank -
coming after the Brisbane and Sydney failures
and the “ home ” runs on the two Chartered
Banks, should finally convince anybody who
needed convincing that the combination of
political with financial scandal in Victoria has
brought Australia to its present pass. The
Parliament which allowed the Siiiels Govern-
ment to exist Just so long as it would do nothing
in the notorious Davies and Munro matters,
sowed seeds of panic in Great Britain as well as
on its own soil. And now the harvest is be-
ginning to be reaped. Englishmen and Scotch-
men do nob draw nice distinctions between the
different divisions of Australia. If is enough
for them that the name of the country has a
bad smell in the money-market, and when their
deposits in any Australian bank fall due they
will promptly run to catch them. They will
presently begin, also, to howl for the liquida-
tion of every bank or society which closes its
door for a minute on sixpen’orth of British
cash ; and Australia having acquired the vice
of making a constant convenience of the foreign
pawnbroker, will become as wretched for a
time as a Chinaman suddenly cut off from his
opium. All because Victorian public life has a
cancer on the soul.

My Dear Moorabinda—
The Sydney Fresh Air League ball is given up.
The Acting-Governorcss blocks the way. She
“could not conscientiously attend a hall during
this season of depression;” but one would think
that a charity ball was a horse of another colour.
And if these are acting-vice-regal sentiments, it
seems passing inconsistent for Chief Justice
Darley to patronise a race-meeting in the said
season of sack-cloth.
I am afraid, from what I read in the papers,
that “the lodger” kills a married woman more
often than her husband does. The “old man”
gets resigned to his fate. No, that isn’t it quite.
The husband will not live with the lady, and the
lodger can’t live without her. “Let us part,” she
says, and immediately the inane locum tenens
commits murder and suicide, where the lawful
possessor says, under the circumstances, “You
bet!” “ How like is woman to a bank deposit !”
said a man to me the other day. “If you can
claim her at any time, you don’t want her. She
would be a troublesome thing to have about the
house. But if she threatens to suspend payment,
you must have her, or shoot somebody.”

Regarding the startled shriek of Sydney society
over the recent marriages of two well-known
youths with a couple of smart barmaids —I must
say I really don’t quite see the propriety of the
howl. Society holding up its fat, white hands in
a did-you-ever attitude over the marriage-certifi-
cate of Hebe looks to me like society defying its
grandmother or some other female progenitor.
The number of ex-barmaids who write their names
with a fine flourish in vice-regal visiting-books all
over the continent is legion, and the admission of
two more into the social ranks is really of small
moment when you come to think it over.
1 have received a novel, “ Mrs. Danby Kaufman
of Bays water,” by an Australian authoress, whom
I will not name till she gives a better account of
herself. “Mrs. D. K. of B. ” is a story which
alludes to “ the rank and fashion of Victoria” in
a reverent spirit; also, it is a book in which the
Melbourne Cup is run before lunch. Discrepancies
of this kind and other drawbacks make “Mrs.
Danby ” not exactly the novel of the season.

A cricket match between the Lyceum company
and the N.S.W. Artillery came off at the Barracks
last Thursday, the actors going out in “Million of
Money ” style on a four-horse coach. As usual, the
most correctly-attired cricketer on the stage side
—the one with the immaculate flannel pants and
the dazzling shirt—got bowled for a duck before
he had time to warm the wicket. ’Twas ever thus
—the infallible hall-mark of inefficiency is a rush
of clothing to the figure.
Talking about fog-horns (though I really don’t
think, I was), the first night Lady Carington spent
on Australian soil she was much surprised at the
screaming of a “ syren ” on the harbour. It was
Lady C.’s only little story, and a very mild one at
that. “Bob asked mein the morning whether I
had heard the kangaroos howling round the house,
and do you know ” (she would say innocently) “ I
really believed him ?” But if Bob, had only said
the racket was the baffled shriek of Potts Point
trying to force the front door, he would probably
have been an undiscovered wag to this very
moment. It would have been so hideously
probable.

who have a show for North Melbourne.
The Queensland elections are practically
over. At time of writing there remain hut two
single-seat electorates to be filled —Carpentaria
and Cook. Out of the 72 members-elect (reserving
the chance that the “ contingent vote ” may alter
the personnel of a couple of seats) Labour has 1G
representatives, who may probably he relied upon
1 1 act as one unit, and to stand no nonsense, with
a maximum sympathetic vote of 21. MTlwraith.
at present figures, may be credited with a toler-
ably blind following of 44, since he (as well as the
Bulletin) probably counts as his very own several
transparent frauds who crept into public tolerance
under the guise of “ Independents.”

Alexandra Variety Show.
Brough and Boucicault’s new comedy,
“The Late Lamented,” is a piece with an old and
conspicuous moral, the burden whereof is that the
fat, sanctimonious man can’t be trusted, for there
is duplicity in his grease. The Lamented himself
is dead ere the comedy begins, therefore he does
not appear in the flesh. Bub ho has left two
widows to mourn ins loss, Both of them marry
again, but both continue to snuffle dismally over
the good man’s grave, and plant flowers thereon,
at which both their husbands feel rather bored ;
and both preserve the picture of the deceased,
which is hung up conspicuously in the drawing-
room. This, by the way, is a feminine peculiarity.
You don’t find men parading the portraits of their
dead wives before their new wives. When a
fellow’s wife dies he buries her and there is an
end of the matter. The Late Lamented is exceed-
ingly like Thomas MTlwraith with his face
covered with dough. The two ladies live
in adjacent flats, therefore it is obvious that they
must one day come in collision. The natural result
is that the Lamented’s reputation utterly goes to
pieces, and both his widows jump furiously at his
portrait, which becomes a discredited oil-horror,
and a thing to be sold by auction without reserve.
The comedy is of French origin ; the dialogue is
distinctly French—particularly in regard to a
certain item concerning a bedstead “full size;”
and Mr. Brough says “damn” on no less than
seven occasions, and denies once that he said
“dash.” It is a sparkling production, with
several powerful situations, and about the best
feminine comedy part The Bulletin remembers is
that in which Mrs. Brough revels. Titheradge has a
lamentably small character as a friend with spasms
and a jelly-mould on his head. Boucicanlt’s damp
and wretched butler, however, makes up for many
things—even for the waste of Miss Brenda Gibson
whom some Vandal has turned into a cook with
practically nothing to do except fail to find Mr.
Brough’s hat, and for the “damn,” and in some
degree for the bedstead. And possibly the “ Late
Lamented ” is one of those comedies which would
be stale and unprofitable if the “ damn ” were
omitted; and at the worst the audience can
always look blankly unconscious when it bears
about the bedstead.
* * He *
& ‘ Slaves of Sydney ” came to an abrupt
but not premature end at the Royal, last Friday,
its death being an awful example to the dissipated
melodrama which goes wrong when it is young
and remains that way when it grows old. The
“ Green Lanes of England,” which arose in its
stead, is a good deal lessjrauddled than the late
lamented production, and does not look so much
like a pork-created nightmare or an opium-dream.
Mr. Dampier’s Gentleman George is a character
whom it is always pleasant to meet, despite the
said George’s constant desire to recite the “ Charge
of the Light Brigade”—an awful and Bullish
piece of verse which ought to get a long and well-
earned rest. Mr. J. H. Rainford’s red, corn-fed
parent k about as gratifying as that unavoidable
evil can be, though The Bulletin would very
much like an angular and bony father for a change;
and Mr. Plimraer, who is settling down to be the
chronic villain of the company, is a rather pleasant
kind of villain, inasmuch as he never laughs the
hollow, mocking laugh of the profession—that
dreadful cacophonous cackle which advertises the
bad man just as the yell of the Cochin-China hen
does the advent of a fresh egg. As for Miss Lily
Dampier, her old fate has overtaken her once
more, and she puts up with it with the grace that
is born of long practice. For the five hundredth
time or thereabouts she is thrown out and cursed
by her heavy parent,, and goes weeping into a cold
and horrid world.. Miss Dampier has been
thrown out in this manner by eighteen different
fathers, who have thrown her an aggregate dis-
tanee of seven miles, and, in common justice, The
Bulletin thinks she ought to throw out the
father now for a change. The spectacle of the
stern, bass parent being ejected in his top-boots
would be a refreshing variation.
*****
Sydney Gaiety, once a steadily prosperous
little theatre, but nearly forgotten of late years,
was announced to re open last Wednesday—just
too late for notice in this issue—with “Rip Van
Winkle,” Mr. George Dean Doing boss and high-
priest of the show. Mr. Dean, like the Gaiety,
has been partially forgotten in Sydney through
long absence, nevertheless he used to have a repu-
tation which would float a much worse company
than the one he brings along with him, and a
much worse opera than “Rip Van Winkle.” His
last appearance at the Gaiety was when that
memorable but awful Arnurrikan lady, Miss Dora
Wiley, closed up her show abruptly and wont into
frantic hysterics on the back premises, and Mr.
Dean’s efforts to keep order in front, where the
audience wanted to wreck the house, and, at the
same time, to resuscitate the insensible lady
behind, are still favourably remembered by many
people who got their money back.
* * * *
‘•‘East Lynne,” at Sydney Her Majesty’s,
if it has done nothing else, has at least demon-
strated that Miss Lilia Wilde may yet become a
great actress. A lady who can play Lady Isabel
without being in the least degree ridiculous, and
without ranting or sniffing dismally over her
fallen fortunes, and yet please the gallery which
came expressly to hear her rant and sniff, has lots
of possibilities before her. At the same time,
there is a certain holy joy in the knowledge that
“East Lynne” will he no more after this week,
and its place will be taken by Charles Reade’s
“ It’s Never too Late to Mend.” The gorgeously-
prssented “A Million of Money,” at the Lyceum,
is also doing its last nights.
* * * *
Mr. Rickards, at Sydney Tivoli, made a
greater effort than usual last week in considera-
tion of Victoria’s birthday, and the audience
acknowledged his endeavours by consistently re-
calling him seven times each night, until the brim
of his shiny hat was worn to a state of excessive
flabbiness with much salaaming to his admiring
constituents. *

“ The Vicar of Bray ” gave his first plea-
sant Saturday evening at Melbourne Princess’,
with cheering r.eft&Ugk Although the libretto is
credited to Sydney immdy, it closely follows the
story which another man wrote to Edward Solo-
mon's music, 12 years ago. It is more humorous,
however, and the composer also seems to hav«
reconstructed his score on sound principles. “Thu
Vicar of Bray ” is an opera of clerical and Sunday,
school interest, but its sentiment is worldly, and
its behaviour riotous—suggesting a Young Men’s
Christian Association on the razzle-dazzle. Bor
instance, the chorus to one song is an unctuous
parody on church music, the last word being
intoned with all the solemnity due to the
“Amen” of psalmody. The name-character
consents for a time to have his views
broadened by a corps de balfct, and becomes
the moving spirit in a church-and-leg
facially, he resembles the cherubic Dr. Bevan’
but, in other respects, he goes much further
than that excellent man. His dancing leans
towards levity, and his fatherly love for the ballet
is saddened by memories ot the dear old time when
they wore their skirts shorter. At tire outset the
Vicar so shocks his curate, Henry Sandford, that
the earnest young evangelist bids farewell to the
Sunday-school children, and drops a tear on the
lady-teachers, before going far away to convert
the heathen. Ultimately, Sandford returns to the
Village of Bray in a more secular frame of mind.
Having been himself converted by a well-meaning
cannibal, he has abandoned the pernicious prac-
tice of talking in rounded periods, Also
before he went abroad he had religious scruples
about; kissing the Vicar’s daughter ; but now
ho hugs her to their mutual satisfaction.
A bevy of other curates, demoralised by the sur-
roundings, offer their cold, flabby hands in
marriage to the ballet; but, being refused by the
leg-artistes, who evince a preference for some
pedestrian fox-hunters in scarlet coats, the curates
fulfil their destiny by taking the lady-teachers.
Then the vicar returns, like a lamb that has
strayed, to the elderly embrace of a certain
widow, and the entire opera subsides into the
sacred bonds of matrimony, with the exception of
the infant Bible-class.
• # #
The climax is ineffective, hut the show,
taken altogether, is lively, and contains many
droll bits of business. In a musical sense,
“The Vicar of Bray’’ is the antithesis of
“The Mountebanks.” Its melodies are simple
and pretty, rather than vague and “ graceful.”
The new tenor, Joseph Tapley, was a success,
vocally and otherwise, in the character of Sand-
ford. There is something of Percy Lyndall about
his curate impersonation, but his manner is more
sterile than Lyndall’s; he has a good quality of
voice, and, when he sings a descriptive song, not a
word is lost. As a stage-lover, Tapley promises
to take precedence of the beauteous Eyley— in
fact, the latter, who has had a long monopoly of
female affection, may be Said to have sat in the
dress-circle to witness his own funeral on Saturday
night. It is a world - wide, world - old rule
that when the handsome tenor comes in at
the door, the baritone has to fall out of the
window, as far as the female portion of the audi-
ence is concerned. George Lauri scores again as
the Vicar, particularly in the dancing department,
Clara Thompson comes well to the front as a giddy,
girly widow of advanced age; while Florence
Young sings well in the part which Effie Clements
did not stop to perform. Howard Vernon strug-
gles unsuccessfully with a stupid part; and Violet
Varley has little to do, except to dance, Violet,
however, is the one member of the ballet who can-
not dance; fortunately, the others supply the
deficiency, and the occasional tripping on of the
girls, after the style of the fairies in “lolanthe,”
gives the necessary touch to the ensemble. Goatcher
has painted two beautiful rural scenes.
** * *
Melbourne Opera House, otherwise the
Alhambra—and why Alhambra nobody knows—
still gets along as well as can be expected at the
price. A variety show that has any sort of in-
clination towards ballet must go to heavy expense
if “legs” are to be done properly, and a music-
hall tariff would barely stand the financial strain,
even if the house filled every night. The Alhambra
does not fill every night—with an accent on the
“ not.” Sometimes it presents the hopeless
appearance of one that has tired of struggling
against adversity, though on Saturdays the case
usually looks more hopeful, and the back benches
smile again. At present the first part is played
down to the waning craze for Football, the show
being advertised to kick off at 8 p.m.

strangers, known as the Crook-Lyle Comedy Com-
pany, made a first attempt on Saturday in “ The
Strategist.” The avowed purpose of this affair is
to supply 1000 laughs in 150 minutes, and a notice
is cautiously withheld. The Gaiety has given
itself over to Professor Hoffman’s mind-reading
entertainment. Another professor is giving
“manifestations” at the Waxworks, each pro-
fessor being emphatic in his opinion that all other
professors are mere vanity and sounding bras>.

the prevailing tendency to humiliation an
prayer.

the national standard as poseurs. The whole cere-
monial reminded us of an occasion when, standing
in the Place de la Concorde, Paris, we saw a pro-
cession of men coming along, all solemnly attired
in black, with draped hats and scarfs, and bearing
an enormous wreath. All their countenances wore
looks of doleful resignation and great solemnity.
They advanced towards the statue of Gambetta,
and then and there poured out a flood of rhetoric.
After everyone had spoken twice, the wreath was
placed on the corner of the pedestal of Gambetta’s
statue, and, solemnly bowing to each other
with raised hats, the processionists dispersed, evi-
dently quite satisfied with themselves.

A concert programme, of 1870, shows A. S. Sullivan,
now Sir Arthur, as pianist. He dropped the S. because
the initials made A.S.S.
Two amusing Melbourne successes are Lake’s Exhibition
of Pictures and Snazelle’s Alhambraic Sundays. Snazzy
was got at by some of the “ piebalders,” and induced to
issue a ukase recommending pianissimo applause, but
then the whole-hoggers got at him, and he made a
speech, inviting the fortissimo.
The warmth of Appleton’s reception, at his Melb.
benefit, was extraordinary. He was an indispensable
Melbourne actor 20 years ago, though somewhat hard,
jerky and stilted, and never of Titheradge’s calibre. He
would have become a barrister, but for his growing
family. We believe he came from Heathcote, as an
amateur, and somewhere about his first Melb. appearance
was at Coppin’s wooden Pantheon, Cremorne Gardens, as
Rodomont Rollingstone, in an old farce.
“Mr. Bellew was admirable as the perplexed husband,”
says an American newspaper criticism. Not his ordinary
r6le, you know.
T. S. Bellair died at Wagga a few days ago. After some
English stage experience, he came to Melbourne about
1858, when he was engaged by Dick and Fred Younge, in
their management of Coppin’s Olympic. He was in the
firm of Lambert, Vincent, Harwood, Stewart, Bellair, and
Hennings, which took over the Royal, when Holt and
Hoskins gave it up, as successors to Barry Sullivan. His
proper name was Prout. For many years he kept the
Pastoral Hotel, Newmarket, nob far from Flemington
Racecourse. Bellair was as stately a man as ex-Thespian
Pollock, of Her Maj.’s.
The mysterious disappearance of Miss during
the past six months has been explained, says a London
correspondent, and she is expected to be about again
within the next few weeks. During her enforced retire-
ment she has been residing in the South of France.
Mr. Edward Terry, who appears in Sydney shortly, is
an excellent actor of the old - fashioned school, and
founded his style on that of J. L. Toole. He is principally
remembered as one of the famous quartette (Nellie
Barren, Kate Vaughan, E. W. Royce, and himself) in the
Gaiety burlesques of 15 years ago.
Pattie Browne, says a gushing Bulletin correspondent,
has fairly “knocked ’em” in Pinero’s new play, at the
Court, and a great deal of interest will be attached to the
next piece in which she is announced to appear. No
stranger has made such a hit on the London stage since
Florence St. John took the town by storm as Madame
Favart, in 1879.
Amongst the newly-elected members of the Green Room
Club are Haddon Chambers and Frank Thornton. Over
60 names are still down for election. The club was founded
in ’77, and at first limited to 100 members ; out of this
little band of originals some 30 only remain—among them
Arthur Garner. The club now numbers 300, and is one of
the most flourishing in London. Irving, Bancroft, Toole,
Pinero, James, Grossmith, Righton, &c., were amongst
the founders.
Johnny O’Neill, Irish comedian, has so much respect
for his Mother Church that, when playing “ Con, the
Shaughraun,” he won’t say, “ The priest give you abso-
lution for that,” but renders it—“ A blessing for that.”

(24th) to produce Tom Taylor’s “ Ticket of Leave Man ”
£7O. Could any ghost walk under that penalty these
hard times ?
A Melbourne actress, subpoenaed as a wdtness in the
recent theatrical assault case, said —“ This is a better
advertisement than a divorce-case.” Opinions differ.
A Clifton Hill (Vic.) 8.0. priest insists upon previously
reading and revising the plays which an amateur dramatic
society proposes to perform. He objected in toto to
Michael Feeney in “ Arrah Na Pogue ” ; cut out the
whole of the great wake scene, and some of the “dis-
graceful lines” put in the mouth of Harvey Duff, in
“The Shaughraun ” ; strongly objected to the character
of Danny Mann, and altogether abolished Father Tom, in
“The Colleen Bawn” ; and wouldn’t hear of “Not such
a fool as he looks” being played at all.
A pleasant item is that Alf. Boothman and his wife
have become reconciled.
Thomas P. Hudson (Mr. May Habgood) announces that
he has dropped minstrelsy for ever, and will please be
known in future as a “ theatrical entrepreneur ” having
inaugurated the “Australian Bureau OoncertCo.” Madame
Antoinette Sterling is trump-card No. 1, but already
negotiations are proceeding with other celebrities.
The clever Tommy, in a word, has been gilded with
refined gold by his accomplished wife.
Simonsen, when travelling with the Italians, told the
Dunedinites that it was a nigger-show they wanted.
They did not like it, and when he tried them with the
colour they would not turn up. The co. only played four
nights with but a few pounds in the house at last.
Portraits of Snazelle and Miss Snazelle hang in the
vestibule at Melbourne Opera House as advts. for his
“Rational Sunday Evening ” entertainment. A few days
ago Snazzy' was seized by a horrible dread that these
portraits might seem to implicate his family in Frank
Clark’s variety show. He hired a fire-engine to rush him
down to the Opera House at full gallop and slapped slips
of paper across G.H.S. and daughter—“ Sunday Nights
Only.” The result is a growing impression that Bill
Adams has turned evangelist.

£480) per night. She has also been re-engaged to appear
ln London in 1894.

performed by the Princess’ resident poet, a chorus lady.
This fair bard established her reputation by winning a

They should now get her to compose a Hospital ballad,
