Prom Players of the Period: “With the advent of
Irving, culture killed conventionality and claptrap;
intellectual distinction triumphed over commonplace
dulness;and brain proved its superiority alike to the
banality of burlesque, the soulless splendour of spectacle,
and the mania and mouthings of melodrama.” It reads
like a hunk from that fine old literary democrat, E. W.
O'Sullivan, of the bell-topper and yellow boots. But the
advent of Irving evidently did not assassinate artfully and
artistically alliterative asininity.
Virginia Earl, when in Sydney, hurt one hand badly
through the explosion of a soda-water bottle. News now
comes from America that amputation has unhappily been
found necessary.
In Wellington (M.L.) has just been exhibited an auto-
matic electric piano.” The inventor lived for several
years in Christchurch (M.L.) under the specious guise of
a kindly-mannered man. He ought to be demanded deli-
very of by a frenzied populace, and solemnly boiled alive.
Fillis’ circus took £3250 in Dunedin in six days.
A London paper prints the once-famous Isidore Wer-
theimer’s epitaph ; “ The late Duke of Manchester prac-
tically died of music - hall mania, and now Isidore
Wertheimer, at the age of only 29, has succumbed to it.
Poor Isidore’s claim to fame (apart from the Dunlo
divorce-suit) lay in the fact that he was the most music-
hally ‘dude’ in London. He wasn’t a ‘chappie’—he
couldn’t live up to being a ‘chappie.’ His friends
called him a ‘ doode ’ or ‘ good ole Izzy.” His
enemies called him—unmentionable things. Isidore
Wertheimer lived in the music-halls and spent his money
on the ‘artistes.’ When he wasn’t at the ‘Pav.’he was
at the ‘Troc.’ or the ‘Tiv.’ Where the pale young
Polish Jew spent his days no one knew. Most folks
opined in bed. About a year ago Wertheimer was de-
clared bankrupt, and announced his intention of quit-
ting the *Pav.’ and the ‘Troc.’ for the Antipodes.
Before, however, this dread threat could be carried into
execution his grandfather, old Daddy Wertheimer, the
renowned bric-a-brac merchant of Bond-street, died, and
left the scapegrace £60,000. Upon this sum Isidore,
for a time, blossomed forth in longer frock coats,
shinier hats and huger camellias than ever. But his
constitution couldn’t stand it any longer, and he died—of
no visible disease except music-hall.”
Had the money been voted by N.S.W.
Parliament to Premier Dibbs for his trip to Eng-
land, the official assignee would have claimed it.
At all events, the Auditor-General and official
assignee would have spent it in law in ascertaining
where it should go.
The Austrian Grand Duke attended ser-
vice at St. Mary’s Cathedral (Sydney) last Sunday
and was given a chair all to himself. But he
didn’t look half as distinguished as Toohey, the
brewer. That gentleman, embossed with Papal
and other decorations, was taken by numbers for
the Imperial foreigner, and when some of these
enjoyed a near view of him they remarked to one
another: “He looks every inch a king ; ain’t he?”
At the Bank of Victoria's first reconstruc
tion meeting, the indignant Enabling, M.L.C., ex-
plained how the trouble had arisen. The bank
“held .£1,200,000 in current accounts, and when
Saturday morning came, and the miserable hounds
who wanted to save their paltry pence ”
Embling’s oration here terminated, amid “great
uproar,” and all Enabling could get in afterwards
was a highly injudicious admission that the “ gold
reserve” had succumbed to a run on current
accounts. An account current, seemingly, must
only flow one way—into the bank. If the tide
turns, over goes the show.
From a sermon on the Commercial Bank
of Australia failure in London Referee ;
“ The Devil did a fine stroke of business when he first
sent money into the world, and by money I do not mean
wealth in kind, or even gold and silver, but that vague
thing which 1 will call bank money, or the money of the
money market. This sort of money consists of neither
coin nor notes—it would need a political economist to say
what it consisted of ; it is something too impalpable to be
defined by the ordinary observer. Anyhow, this is the
sort of money which I regard as the curse of society,
because without it the accumulation of great fortunes
would be impossible. Perhaps I should make my mean-
ing clearer if I defined my pet aversion to be not money,
but finance. ... It is not money I inveigh against,
taking the word in its simple sense, but the practice of
storing values upon paper—in other words, finance, or
banking.”
The paper-made city man can snort at these
untechnioal remarks, but Australia ought to paste
the paragraph upon its soul, as an antidote to
those erudite lectures which “ experts,” like Gyles
Turner, have been wont to deliver upon the “ Art
of Banking,” or the “Secret of Finance.”

When the Tongau Parliament opened it
was announced that 20 damsels would be picked as
waitresses; but the first 20 that offered were taken,
because the word went round that the rest of the
shoal of applicants would otherwise suicide.

“Sir Galahad himself would have no
chance with a jury now,” writes a Melb. paper, in

Some half-dozen Hebraic accommodation-
gentlemen of Melbourne, on comparing notes last
week, were painfully surprised to find that they
had each been landed with a “family heirloom,”
in the shape of “ruby” necklets of precisely
similar pattern. The lady raised from £3O to £5O
apiece on them three months ago (under pledges
of the strictest secrecy), to enable her to hurry
“ home” and claim a fat inheritance. The fact of
the stones being only ruby-garnets, and practically
valueless, w-as only discovered on one of the lenders
asking £2O advance on his security from a Mel-
bourne pawnbroker. There is at present a great
glut in the Southern ruby market.
“George Manton, recently arrested-for
the murder of Flora M‘Donald, at Marlborough
Station, admitted that he killed the girl because
she refused to marry him. He killed her with an
axe as she lay in bed. Manton then attempted to
burn the body as it lay upon the bed. The body
was considerably charred and the bed nearly all
burnt.” Such is love. The man who doesn’t chop
un the girl who has refused to marry him, so
as to prevent her marrying anybody else, simply
doesn’t love her. He may imagine he does, but
he doesn’t all the same. The man who unsuccess-
fully loves a woman and yet lets her go off alive,
is a failure as a lover—from a newspaper point of
view, anyhow.
If the Employers’ Liability Act embraced
the bantam officers of “ our ” Auxiliary Squadron,
someone would have to answer for the terrible
deaths of H.B.M.S. Lizard’s firemen, resulting
from an accident on the 9th May. As it is there
is an evident desire to get over the disagreeable
matter, and have it done with. The story, as
first told, was too blindly ridiculous to stand a
day: “It appears from the statement of the
officers that, after leaving Jervois Bay, the wind
suddenly veered round and drove the flames up
the furnace doors, thereby terribly burning
Brenchly, Gale, and Dawson.” With the Mildura
disclosures fresh in mind, and some idea of little
hells arranged for the firemen on the vessels of the
squadron by the forced-draught system, the
people are not likely to be satisfied without a full
and free investigation. Which means that they
are not likely to be satisfied, anyhow.
Victoria, by the grace of God, had a birth-
day this week, and in consequence the mails were
stopped (The Bulletin thus has to publish a day
earlier), almost every place of business was shut
up, the banks were closed, the railway service was
dislocated, and many thousands of people lost a
day’s work and a day’s pay which they could ill
afford, and consequently cursed Queen Victoria
from the bottom of their hearts. There is alto-
gether too much Queen’s Birthday and Albert
Edward’s Birthday in Australia. In Britain the
occasion passes almost unnoticed, and instead of
stopping all business, things go on just as
usual, and the inhabitants even shift the holiday
about to prevent it clashing with the local pig-
market, or any similar festivity. Probably it is
because Victoria is so far off that Australia suffers
from such a grovelling rfeverence for her birth-
day. The thing that is far off is always beautiful,
whether it is Victoria, or Heaven, or Paraguay,
or anything else. Someone has even been writing
of late about “Happiness in Hell,” which is
a case exactly in point.
Bibliographers everywhere, and a wide
circle of personal friends, will regret to hear of
the early retirement of Mr. R. 0. Walker,
Public Librarian of Sydney. The proceed-
ing will not be in consequence of the
retrenchment policy of the Dibbs Government,
but will result from the fact that the longer Mr.
Walker remains in the Public Service the less will
be his pension. It will be a severe blow to the
interests of Australian bibliography and history—
and the political and material interests of these
provinces are more closely affected by a wide
diffusion of knowledge respecting our past than
most people imagine—if Mr. Walker is permitted
to retire before his many years’ special research
and intermittent work are brought to a
conclusion by the publication of the great cata-
logue of the Free Public Library. The preparation
of the Melbourne Public Library catalogue has
been the work of a special staff, but in Sydney the
undertaking has had to be performed by Mr.
Walker, with the aid of his assistants, who are
chiefly occupied with the routine work of the
institution, and the Principal Librarian’s own
time has thus been trenched upon to an enormous
extent. The section of tne catalogue relating to
Australasia will pr;>ve of paramount importance,
and its issue is being anticipated with interest by
the librarians of the world.

The publication by Francis Adams of
Griffith’s ’BB election manifesto, with its denuncia-
tion of land-grab railways and black-labour as an
appendix to “the Australians” by way of illus-
trating Australian Liberalism, is what the French
call “a bleeding irony” in the light of recent
events. But it was dme seriously and only shows
that Adams has already lost touch with Australia.
An American court has decided that if a
meteor falls on a piece of rented property, that
natural and sometimes valuable curiosity belongs
to the landlord, and not to the tenant. But it
hasn’t decided that if the aerolite hits the tenant
in its descent he can sue the landlord for damages
received from the landlord’s meteor, the same
being at large and wandering around without
proper control.
After years of fooling with infernal
machines for closing carriage doors, including
handles worked by the engine and bolts manipu-
lated from the guard’s van, the Vic. railways have
practically surrendered to a patent lock invented
by one Busby, a local engineer. The Busby lock
is a imple, economical affair, the door-handles
being the key, a double-catch and a single move-
ment admitting of no misgivings as regards the
boy who leans against the door with his head out
of window. It is intended to abolish the porter
who walks along the platform turning handles after
they are ostensibly all right. In this case the door
can t close without being securely fastened.
The attempt to drive female compositors
out of Maoriland newspaper offices, though under-
standable under all the existing circumstances,
cannot succeed, and would afford no advantage to
the trade if it did succeed. The open alternative
is to admit women to the typographical unions.
So far, the competition of women among the.
printers in Maoriland has so reduced wages that,
just now, a husband and wife, both compositors,
working in the same office, only manage to earn as
much together as a male compositor can earn
solus in Sydney. The fear of those who wool 1
exclude females from typist-unions is that so
many women would qualify for membership that
they would exert a majority vote and lower the
union rate of wages all round. Even with this
danger ahead, their admission is preferable to
their exclusion, under which they are now exert-
ing an outside vote (so to speak) and airing a
grievance which evokes public sympathy.
The cablegrams relating the peculiarly
Gallic welcome accorded in France to the returned
African warrior, Dodds, reek with a sour odour of
inverted manhood. “ The populace scattered
flowers in his path !” Why not blood-bedraggled
bullocks’ livers? Popular applause seems to be
the veritable breath of life to a large proportion of
humanity, though, in the abstract, it might be
imagined that a stern warrior, moulded after the
canons of Spartan simplicity, would just as soon
be hit across the mouth with a dead cat as
showered with rose-leaves. The scene must have
been anidealistical enlargement of a disgusting in-
cident witnessed in Sydney the other day, when a
fat, bald-headed old fellow, bidding farewell to an
adult male passenger by one of the Messageries
Maritimes boats, publicly and theatrically kissed
his friend fairly under the moustache.
The big libel case of Speight v. Syme is
looming amongst next month’s possibilities upon
the Melb. legal horizon. Mitchell, reckoned,
perhaps, the soundest common-lawman at the Vic.
Bar, was to have been Speight’s senior counsel;
but he has had to give up work and go on a health-
tour. Duffy, who takes his brief, with Coldham
as junior, will have an opportunity of renewing
the warfare carried on in the Clara Parker case
against Purves, Q.C., who holds a general retainer
for the Age. The mantle of Madden, who was
associated with him, and conducted the prelimi-
naries of the defence till his elevation to the 0.J.-
ship, will probably fall upon Deakin, who has
torn himself away from irrigation and Imperial
federation for the more lucrative practice of the
law, at which he is now flourishing.
Dear Bulletin, —I cheerfully plead guilty
to the charge of supporting the “ Australasian
Rights Purchase Bill” now before Parliament,
and only wish that all our “ natural opportunities ”
in the shape of city lots, coal-lands, harbour and
river frontages, &c., had, in time past, been
“ grabbed ” in similar fashion. The company
in question proposed to lease a “natural oppor-
tunity” in the shape of the water-power of the
Grose and Colo Rivers, leaving to the community
the right to re-assess the rental at fixed periods,
or to take over the whole of the electric power
plant which the company proposes to erect, after
the expiration of 15 years, at its actual cost. All
“unearned increment” in either case reverts to
the State. As this method of dealing with
“ natural opportunities ”• is precisely that which I
have consistently advocated for many years, I
offer no apology for the course I have taken. —I
am, yours truly, Fhank Cotton. P.S. By the
way,'it was “Jim Mooney,” not myself, who
headed the famous strike-procession in shearer’s
costume. —F.O.
THOSE MISERABLE HOUNDS.
We sing about the animals
Who, having scant belief
In balance-sheets and capitals,
Come slinking like a thief,
To steal away on Saturday
Their dirty pence and pounds,
As though a decent bank would pay
Those miserable hounds 1
Why do they want their money out—
Why don't they let it stand?
Why should they entertain a doubt
Concerning “ cash in hand ?”
Why can’t they close their canine ears
When Panic’s trumpet sounds,
Instead of giving way to fears ?
Those miserable hounds 1
If no one needed rhino “ for
To meet ” his weekly bills,
A bank would never shut its doors,
However bare its tills.
11 Suspension ” is a word unknown
Where confidence abounds,
But hear them barking for their bone !
Those miserable hounds 1
When broken banks begin again
To reconstruct their wrecks.
They ought to fix an iron chain
Round all their clients’ necks ;
And if the hungry, yelping pack
Grow troublesome —then, zounds 1
The masher clerks must hold ’em back,
Those miserable hounds 1

G. D. Carter, the Victorian Treasurer, was
once lunching at the Austral m Club with John
Gavan Duffy and grumbled at what the waiter
brought him. “ Oh,”said Duffy, “you’d grumble
if you were in Heaven.” “I would!” promptly
replied Carter. “And what would the grievance
in Heaven be ?” asked Duffy. “ That you weren’t
there.”
Mr. Henry Halloran, once wejl-known as
N.S.W. Principal Under-Secretary and as a writer
of more or less graceful verses, died last week,
aged 82. Born in Cape Colony, he came to
N.S.W. in 1827, and was for 51 years in the
local civil service. A year or two ago, he
married, en seronde noccs, a young lady graduate
of Melbourne University.

The celebrated saying of Nick Fitzgerald,
M.L.C. (Vic.) about Money Miller is prigged from
Heine’s “Letters from Berlin”; “The merchant
has the same religion all the world over. His
counting-house is his church, his ledger his Bible,
his gold his God, his credit his faith.” These
purple literary patches are always cribbed from
somewhere. They shine out, like the big diamond
on the dirty finger of the old clo’ man.
A quarter of a century ago Ministers of
Education in Victoria said that Joe Geary was
the best inspector of schools (on paper) in the
service, Once Joseph was called to account for
not having been at a certain place at a particular
time. He replied that there were appalling floods
at Boggy Greek and he remained there a fortnight
to assist in saving the lives of Her Majesty’s
subjects. The explanation was “deemed satis-
factory.”

The Bulletin perceives with much regret
that the open-handed Duke of Edinburgh has
been hard hit by the suspension of the Commercial
Bank of Sydney and by the failure of a London
firm. Could not a “Week of Self-Denial” be
proclaimed throughout Australia to help in re-
plenishing his coffers? The memory of his truly
princely generosity, when on this side of the
world, is still so green that the response would be
most hearty, especially as it is quite certain that
the Duke only invested money in the local bank in
order to encourage a deserving Australian insti-
tution and with no idea of drawing big interest
for his money.

organisation by being interested in a trade-pro-
tection office, of which ostensibly he was only the

union, which Harden had contravened by working
cheaply for his clients, ordered an “enquiry,”
the costs of which, <£94o, Harden, suspended from
practice for a year, was condemned to pay. He
has not paid them, and is now called on to shew
cause why he should not be sent to gaoh

Poor old murdered litigant Slack, who for
30 years bothered Victorian Judges by appearing
in person in “Slack agin Windher, yer Honor,”
and “ Slack agin Dounton, number wan, two, six,
five,” was succeeded by one J. J. Main. Main is
now in Sydney, and may be heard of soon by the
Full Court. He has been in most of the Victorian
courts, and his last (abortive) venture was to try
to establish a title to a big slice of Queen-street,
Melbourne, worth about half-a-million of money.
Respecting a recent Bulletin par. about
the discovery of Mt. Morgan (Q.), a correspondent
writes: “ Morgan does not disguise the fact that
he acted treacherously to his mate Gordon. The
Rockhampton men to whom Morgan’s brother
took the stone to be tested also acted deceitfully,
until Gordon was induced to part with his land at
the nominal price of ill per acre. Gordon’s
widow was left unprovided for, and she is now (or
was very lately) earning her livelihood at the
washtub. It would not be a very great stretch of
generosity if one or other of the Mt. Morgan
potentates allowed the poor woman a small
annuity. Will the Jimmy-Woodser millionaire of
that crowd take a hint ? ”
John Gale, teetotal potentate, ex-parson,
and ex-M.L.A. for the Mumnnbidgee, once more
thinks of wooing an electorate. Gale is probably
the oldest of N.S.W. country pressmen, having
made a competence-out Queanbeyan way long
before the advent of the railway. He is addicted
to poetry, and in his youth published a small
volume in England. Among the debris of the
awful Dunbar wreck washed ashore at South
Head was a copy of this little book, draggled,
salt, and stained, belonging to some ill-fated
passenger unknown, The author afterwards re-
recovered it, and possibly has it still.
In N.S.W. Legislative Council, the other
night, Mr. Jacob, M.L.C., wanted certain business
adjourned because his mind was “unhinged,” as
is unhappily the case with many other citizens, by
bank failures. Talking of unhinged minds there
is one old member of the same Council whose in-
tellect has been wandering for years, during
which he has still voted consistently against
everything in the shape of progress. He generally
dodders in sometime in the early part of the even-
ing, sits awhile with his rheumy, lack-lustre eye
fixed fishily upon the President, then feebly
gathers himself up like a man who collects a
skeleton, bows, and roams out in dead silence. He
doesn’t seem to know anybody, or to have any
idea as to where he is or what ho came for—he
simply gravitates there from force of habit, as
Wilkie Collins’ giantess used to gravitate to the
corner of the room with her cookery-book and
turn an imaginary omelette with a massive hand
in her dreams. A melancholy sight is this oil
member, and truly representative of an Assembly
afflicted with locomotor ataxy of the mind.

D. D. Wheeler, leader of Victorian Han-
sard staff, is to be retrenched as a sexagenarian,
and will be succeeded by E. B. Loughran, who
shines as a poet as well as a shorthand writer.
A letter-carrier in an Upper Darling town
costs N.S.W. Government something like £2OO to
prosecute for stealing a letter containing a 7s. 6d.
sewing-machine instalment. Yet none of the
daily papers howled about the “cost of the prose-
cution ” as they have done about the cost of pro-
secuting Matthew Davies.
Reconstruction.
The prompt acceptance of various schemes of
banking reconstruction is a cheering proof of the
restoration of public confidence in the solvency,
stability and future prospects of the monetary
institutions of New South Wales, and demon-
strates the fact that depositors have recovered
control of their reasoning faculties. The turmoil,
anxiety and worry, inseparable from the panic,
carry their own hygienic penalties—-nerves are un-
strung, strength is diminishing., and the various
organs of the body are thrown into chaotic con-
fusion. To reconstruct the nervous and muscular
systems, and rebuild afresh shaken constitutions,
recourse should be had to that wonderfully effica-
cious and widely-known remedy, Marshalls
Tonic Nervine—a proved “perfect panacea for
all disorders springing from lack of nervous
vitality. Mr. A. A. Blackwood, one of the oldest
and best-known travellers on the Western road,
writes from Dubbo, under date 7th May
Some time ago I used a botlle of Marshall’s Tonic
Nervine, and several since. Just bought another a
G raham’s. I never used anything so effective in renewing
vitality and appetite. I have recommended it to dozen ,
who are using it and state the same, although chemis
laugh at the idea as fancy; but their own quinine a
iron mixtures, which they substitute for it, nearly aiway
affect the head, whilst Marshall’s Tonic Nervine d
not. Unless the article was as I describe it, I would
lend my name to it.

Queen Victoria, seated on a golden throne*
presented her by some toadying Oriental nigger
with pipe-stem legs, opened the Imperial Institute,
the buildings whereof have now been “dedicated ’
by the Archbishop of Canterbury. Dedicated to
what? To a system of complex tyranny that
usurps many of the prerogatives of the Almighty !
To a thieving hierarchy that footpads the mean
savings of dissenting religionists! To the incubus of
royalty that bestra Idles all the smaller tyrannies,
and arrogantly sanctities its own and their plun-
derings with the double seal of snuffledom and
blasphemy,. and this while the gaunt millions of
London and Hull and all Englan 1 offer their
starving bodies to the bayonets of a hireling
soldiery in despair of earning the price of other
carrion. Can Heaven and Hell make a stronger
contrast of rampant magnificence and desiccated
squalour?

<,goal is the Legislative Council.

rolls, and the Labour executive works just as hard

A member of Parliament who is so often
at law over transactions prima facie shady, and
who is eternally escaping the direct effects of

must get into gaol at last. And when he does,

murderer in the Waxworks !”
Premier Patterson announces that his
Government cannot join in any more schemes for
the benefit of Melbourne banks. The Govern-
ment, he finds, is an impotent friend for any
kind of institution to have at its back. As a
restorer of confidence it is not much superior to an
earthquake, he might have added.
The passages of abstruse philosophy on
the “ meejum of exchange” so eloquently marking
the N.S.W. State Bank Parliamentary Com-
mittee’s Report are mainly the workmanship of
Rose and O’Sullivan. The member who knew
most about money contributed least to the
elegance of the document from an oratorical point
of view.

we need not repeat them here. The land-monopoly is the

combating it is by the fair and simple expedient of taxing
the land at a uniform rate according to its value.

financial embarrassment of the State. “ Hand over

A certain Australian Treasurer is a spec-
tacle for gods and men when he appears in the
city streets among a group of bank directors, who
pat him on the back, smile at him, laugh at his
inane jokes, and, finally, hustle him into the bank;
while the Treasurer himself simpers like a young
maiden who has just received her first declaration
of love,

When. Fox moved in the Commons, nearly
a century ago, that “The influence of the Crown
has increased, is increasing, and ought to be
diminished,” lie did not think that the transfer ot
that influence from the sovereign to the Ministry
mffiht in time result in a practically irresponsible
despotism. Yet, that is the actual state of affairs
to-day in every Australian province. A Ministry
with a majority at its back can defy the law.

Britain, according to latest advices, has
its eye on Beloochiatan, which it is considered
necessary to swallow because the Khan is alleged
to be a bad and cruel character who needs to be
suppressed. This is another step towards the
inevitable. Presently Britain, Russia, and China
will own Asia among them, with possibly a side-
show for France in the extreme South-west, and
then the three big empires will concuss against
each other somewhere on the Roof of the World
or thereabouts, with the greatest concussion that
has been heard since the Crusades.
The Pinaroo lands, which the Downer
(S. A.) Govt., with a great flourish of trumpets,
announced in its election policy were to be
opened for settlement, are located somewhere near
the Ninety-mile Desert. The Labour-members
assert that unless they can see this alleged Para-
dise they will vote against the waste of money
involved, and because they propose a personal
inspection by a Parliamentary party, the Govern-
ment, backed by the Ass members, shriek that
“ this is a pretty way to preach economy a
Parliamentary picnic.” The S.A, Labour-mem-
bers arc asking how long it is since this fit of
virtue came over a Government which recently
had to foot a bill for Jc 1500 for a useless Murray
picnic I
N.S. Wales Premier Dibbs’ recantation of
his political opinions did not take his Cabinet
colleagues by surprise. None of them ever believed
him a Republican, and the words uttered by him
giving colour to what they call the “ imputation
they regarded as a mere lapsus lingua. Before
Dibbs set out for England, Minister Slattery
offered to provide him with an opportunity of
defining his relation to the Throne, but the offer
was not accepted. Taken as a whole, no Cabinet
in Australia prides itself more on its grovel-
ling loyalty to Queen Victoria and the Empire
than the present one of N. S. W, Copeland is only
verbally eccentric, like Dibbs, on the topic, while
the rest bow solemnly down iq jfche Awful
Presence.
The law of the North-west Territory
(Canada) provides that the following things shall
be exempt from seizure for debt, except when the
judgment is for the price of the article itself.
Food, furniture and bedding, however, up to the
specified value, are free from seizure under all
circumstances whatever; —
1. Clothing of defendant and family. 2. Furniture and
household furnishings of defendant and family to value of
500dois. 3. Necessary food for defendant’s family for six
months, which may include grain and flour, or vegetables,
aiid meat either prepared for use or oq foot 4. Two
cows, two oxen and one horse, or three horses or mules ;
six sheep and two pigs, besides the animals kept for
food purposes, and food for salne during the six
months beginning' ill November. 5. Harness for
three animals, one waggon or two carts, one mower
or scythe, one breaking plough, one cross plough, one
set harrows, one horse-fake, one sewing-machine, one
reaper and binder. 6. Books of a professional man. 7.
Tools and necessaries used by defendant in trade or pro-
fession. .8. Seed grain sufficient to seed all land under
cultivation hot BO acred (2 bushels to alcre and
14 bushels of potatoes). 9. Homestead up to 80 acres.
10. House and buildings and lot or lots upon which same
are situated, up to the sum of 1500dol. in value'.
We need some such statute in Australia, if only to
prevent the country being swallowed whole by
the reconstructed banks. Let it be said again—
no credit should be given because of the lender’s
or vendor’s power to turn the debtor and bis
family out into the rain.

An ex-patient says (can it possibly be
true?) that as soon as the female convalescents of
a certain Sydney hospital are able to sit up easily
they are set to the pleasing and mirth-provoking
amusement of making shrouds. The woman who
makes most of those cool and cheerful-looking gar-
ments receives an “extra” of some sort —tea,
sugar or butter. But, after all, what employment
is more suitable and natural to a woman than
dressmaking of some sort, especially when the
article can’t get nasty and send it back as a mis-
fit ?
• • •
“ Captain Dodds, of the N.S. Wales
cavalry, is,” says the cable, “to dine with the
Marquis of Ripon.” Dodds is such a swell that
one would hardly expect him to dine with anyone
of lower rank, though it is rather rough on a
potentate of his principles to have to foregather
with a Gladstonian. But a Gladstonian Marquis
is better than none, and Dodds was not so lucky
as Dibbs, He did not find a Conservative Govern-
ment in power.
• * *
Queen Victoria admires the uniform of
the N.S.W. cavalry. It is not generally known
that this uniform was suggested by Capt. J.
Reid, late R. A., of Sydney. The Sydney authori-
ties let the suggestion rest for two or three years,
and then adopted it as their own.
# * #
State and Church conspire to bow down
before His Imperial and Royal Highness Franz
Ferdinand D'Este, heir presumptive to the Aus-
trian throne, now visiting Australia. The N.S.W.
Railway Commissioners have placed a special train
at his disposal gratis, and he appears to have
ordered at St. Mary’s Cathedral an “ Imperial
High Mass ” just as one might order liver-and-
bacon for breakfast. The plea, in at least the
former case, is that the Prince is “ a distinguished
visitor.” To say exactly what he is distinguished
for would be somewhat inhospitable.
# # #
Some of the daily papers have been talk-
ing for some time past of “the Archduke of
Austria ” —as if there were but one Archduke of
Austria, whereas their name is legion. Every
scion of the House of Hapsburg is an Archduke of
Austria, even to the fiftieth cousin fifty times
removed.
A case of “ spoons ”
Inuch in evidence on a coast-
ing steamer in Spencer’s
Gulf (S.A.). A waggish
passenger discovered that
they were going to get mar-
ried at the first port of call,
and he laid the skipper on.
The captain, in sheer awe of
Mother Grundy, offered to
marry the pair there and
then, . . . Done in one
act. . . . Then the wag
seriously informed the skip-
per that he had committed
an illegal act that rendered
him liable to heavy penal,
ties, but offered to see him
through at the first port by
persuading the “bride” to
get “the blessing of a church”
on her union. The scared
skipper, accordingly, at his
own expense, hired a parson
and had the pair married a
second time. . . But the
wag came again and advised
him to consult a lawyer as
to how he had best act to
escape theconsequencesof his
illegal conduct. And then the
child of Neptune paid a son
of Nicholas a fee in order to
learn with assurance that his
own performance of the mar-
riage-ceremony had been as
binding and legal as a
“ bodcomb fence ” —“ horse-
high and pig-tight.”
# • •
The inscription, “ All
meals, threepence,’’ grows
more and more common out-
side Melbourne restaurants.
The man who wants to make
a new departure in the city
of re-construction will have
to start a cook-shop where
all meals are twopence, with
a special halfpenny depart-
ment for the customer who is
willing to pick a bone on the
door-mat.
The following, from a Mildura (Vic.) cor-
respondent to The Bulletin, points several
morals :
Three pumpkins, a dish of tomatoes and a frilled knuckle
of ham, displayed in a window in the prohibitionist “ irri-
gation ” settlement of Mildura, are the outward and
visible signs of an inward and spirituous grace in the
back-parlour cupboard—whisky, 6d. a nobbier; “colonial ”
bottled Leer. Is. Cd. to 2s. a bot tle ; the margin of profit
being considered equal to the risk run of a big fine, with,
perhaps, three moons in quod. There isn’t a pub. or
barmaid in Mildura, but there are about 25 frilled-ham-
bone shops. Their business is considered off the regular
police “bate,” but the excise-officers contract periodical
spasms of zeal. A few months ago, 26 hamhoners were
fined £25 apiece, and some were sent to gaol as well, but,
within a week, the vacancies were filled, and the pumpkin
signals were out as usual. Then, after a nap, the excise
authorities s; nt up a dummy carpenter, who, in the guise
of a beery artisan, collected evidence against 19 more.
The prosecuted persons joined purses, and offered t'6o to a
Bendigo counsel to come and defend them. He came, but
preferred to stay at Gol-gol, on the N.S.VV. side of the
river, where there was a pub. His host made Gol-gol
so attractive that he stayed there while his clients were
being fined and suit off to prison. Those of his
clients who were ordered to Bendigo for change of
diet had to put in a night at the local lock-up, the ladies
among them being accommodated in the troopers’ saddle-
room* whereby hangs a tale. All this happened only a
month ago, but the tomato-shops are once more in full
swing, just as if there had never been any yesterday.
Mildura is one huge farce. It grows grapes, it makes
Wine, and it is proposing to distil brandy. Yet the local
winemaker mustn’t sell less than two gallons in any one
act, and brandy-shops are forbidden. Nevertheless, in
less than three months a local “agent” imported 450
Cases of whisky and beer. Mildura bibbers get all the
drink they want, but pay 50 per cent, more for it than
they ought to. If anyone talks about the need for a
decent hotel he is silenced as a reprobate. That is “ pro-
hibition ” in Mildura.
The Bulletin’s old question to the prohibitionists
here arises—Do you propose to root up the vine-
yards ?

If a list of “ big ” names indebted to the
various “reconstruction” banks were printed
opposite to the amounts they have borrowed —
what a groan Of disgdst and distrust would go up
to Heaven ! The sound of five hundred, or so, fat
men crying “ Shame! ” on the exposure of the
naked truth would not be heard at all. Now, at
a small, and partially packed, meeting the fat
man’s “Shame ! ” suffices to keep everybody else
quiet—

HE: “I used to drive a street watering-cart, madam.”

In Westralia —now deservedly groaning
under a retributive small-pox epidemic—the sister-
in-law of the Premier openly advertises in the
Daily News (Perth) as follows :
~\\J ANTED. —A (rood Chinaman Cook. Apply at
V* once to Mrs. A. Forrest, St. George’s Terrace.
Mrs. A. Forrest is the “Mayoress’’ of Perth>
and her husband is Premier John’s own brother.
From which facts one may estimate the up-to-date
position of the Chinese curse in the West.
« # *
The eccentricities of some Sydney charity
organisations. A hard-up woman came lately
before a board of philanthropists. Hard-up was
represented as being not alone hungry, but almost
naked. One lady undertook to satisfy require-
ments under the latter head, and sent
along a tailor - made dress of her own,
without any other garment whatever. Let it be
added that the Lady Bountiful is 4ft. Bin , and
slim in proportion, and that the poor, but honest
Hardup was sfb. 7in., and somewhat fuller tha 1
Genevieve Ward in “Forget-me-not.” A
married couple, sick and out of work,
had their plight considered by a board.
The management relieved them by a five-
shilling order on Kidman’s. But, as Kidman was
only a grocer, what were they to do for bread ?
After exhausting deliberation, Kidman decided to
give fid. in cash, and 4s. fid. worth in kind. Still
again, a member of a board volunteered to be a
whole Samaritan in himself, and asked that an
unemployed whose case was under discussion
should be sent to his warehouse. The unem-
ployed, a married man, went, whereupon the
Samaritan discharged a £2 ss, a week man and
put the stranger in his place at =£l per week.

The old O’Billabong died and left the
young O’Billabong .£6OO. Wishing to do credit
to the old man’s memory, the young O’Billabong
went into the township for a spree. When his
heart was “tender like” with grog, there came
across his emotional horizon a widdy—a heretic,
with six childher. She married him out of hand,
with the aid of a Wesleyan parson. And now it
is expected that the district priests will all join
the unionists, and crusade against “ freedom of
contract ” and “ scab ” marriages. Ochone !
Public
are the most unequal
of social arrange-
ments. To some, say
Government clerks,
they come as unbough t
luxuries ; to others,
such as ordinary day-
labourers, they mean
deprivation of a day’s
wages, which, in these
days, makes idleness a
misery. To shop-
keepers they invari-
ably spell loss. Then
many people neither
get the holiday nor
are paid for working
on it; while there are
still others whose
work is doubled on
holidays, without any
extra payment.

horses which, in their
prime, carried acouple
the first stage of their

# * •
In Richmond
(Melb.), “each church
has agreed to look
after its own poor.”
This announcement
is published as a sort
of clerical “extra-
ordinary,” and a pattern to the Christian world.
Hitherto the Richmond churches, like all estab-
lishments of the kind elsewhere, have been mainly
concerned in looking after their own rich, and the
new departure is quite a novelty.
# * *
A perusal of Cast Away and Other
Poems, by Charles G. Bond .(published by
Turner and Henderson, Sydney) in poor-rhymed
■efforts and poorer blank spasms, carries the ver-
dict that, when the events which moved the lyre
are stale, even the best topical poetry reads like
the cooey of a lost bogie-man. Here is Mr.. Bond a
preliminary keen on the death of the Duke of
Clarence :
Bow low the head ! the glory
Of our land is darkened, the rippling streams
And woodland lights and cliffs and restless waves
Are darkened with the shadows of Azrael’s
Dreaded wings—even Nature dons her
Sorrowing mantle.
There is no more poetry in this, now that George
is going to marry the disconsolate May, than
there would be in a maintenance affidavit.
# • *
Divorce item, from our own co-respondent.
At Dunedin (M.L.) Mr. Gillfeld has been seeking
a divorce from his late wife —so-called, not because
the lady is deceased, but on account of her staying
out so late at night with another man. Accord-
ing to the evidence, the culminating point in the
troubles of this harassed pair arrived when Gill-
feld found another man in his garden at 1,1 p.m.,
and using huggy-huggy language to his wife
through the kitchen window. I hen the other
man, instead of wisely taking express and
flying from the scene, got into the witness-
box and tried to explain away this awkward cir-
cumstance. The following is the explanation
slightly condensed :
I was there watching Mrs. Gillfeld and the servant girl
There wasn’t any servant girl, hut I expected there would
be one that night. I knew the Gillfelds were about to
engage a servant girl, who, I supposed, would arrive on
the Friday or Saturday night. Mrs. Gillfeld had been
in the office that week, and 1 heard her tell Mr. Gillfeld
that she was going to engage a servant girl. I did not
know that she was going to arrive that night, but 1
thought I would go and see if she was there. I had no-
thing exactly to do with the servant girl, but I went
there to see if anything would happen to her. I oouldn t
see what was going on inside, hut then I didu t know
beforehand whether I would be able to see anything or
not. 1 was outside the window as a friend of the family.
If I hadn’t been there, there wouldn’t have been anyone
there. If Mr. Gillfeld had done anything to the servant
girl I should have interfered that very night.
The co-respondent who explains things too much
is generally lost.

On what principle are subalterns in the
N.S.VV. Artillery allowed £52 a year each for a
horse? In the garrison artillery they do not need
a mount, and the field-battery men should use
battery horses.
* * *
Some years ago a certain Australian officer
was charged with irregularities in connec-
tion with regimental funds. In consequence of
disclosures at an enquiry, the then Govt, ordered
that the officer should never lie promoted. The
whole of the documents were published m a
Parliamentary paper; but it does not appear in
the bound volumes of Votes and Proceedings, and
the officer has since been promoted.
A Coilingwood (Vic.) coffin-artist adver*
tises “every requisite for funerals.” The chiei
requisite for a funeral is a corpse. “Where ar«
the police?”

The British authorities have decided to
award a long-service medal to Australian volun-
teer officers of 20 years’ service. The Imperial
policy varies not. To control the rank-and-file of
every class of society—bebauble the leaders.

The conduct of a Palmerston (M.L.) meet-
ing of creditors towards a local insolvent marks a
new departure in bankruptcy procedure. In this
ARITHMETICAL WRATH.
“Why don’t you hit him back?”
“Well, I promised mother that I’d always count a hundred when I’m
angry. But wait till I’ve finished counting—then you’ll see
things move around.”
c ase the whitewashee laid down .£3 on the table,
which amount he declared to be his sole assets.
But somehow the meeting viewed the statement
with suspicion, and proceeded to undress him and
go through his clothes, whereupon he panned out
for an additional sum of £G Bs. Then, while in a
nude state, he offered an unsatisfactory explana-
tion that the money was his wife’s private pro-
perty, and thereupon someone suggested that
the lady might also crush for a satisfactory
yield per ton if she could be examined before her
husband arrived home to give her warning. This
idea was accepted with acclamation, and the in-
solvent was detained while an official was des-
patched to interview his wife. The local paper
draws a veil over the horrors which ensued, but
presumably the wife was examined by two brawny
females and her clothes ripped up in search of
hidden wealth, while the male 'official looked for
buried treasure in the back-yard. The moral
is that the insolvent nowadays had better put
all his plunder into one big bank-note, and hide
the note inside the porous-plaster on his back.
And even then there is just a chance that the
official assignee may soak the bankrupt in hot
water till the plaster comes off.
* * *
Pastor George Lewis, who, in connection
with the Sussex-street Mission Church, Sydney,
gives the unemployed daily penny meals and free
Sunday breakfasts, and bns an idea of furnishing
them with penny winter “dosses,” crystallises a
fin-de-siticle, practical idea thus :
My ideal of a modern church is to have the basement
floor fitted up for such work as this (the feeding business),
the next floor for Sunday-school purposes, then the
church proper, and, above all, on the top floor, a sort of
hospital for the poor.
This is dreadfully heterodox. A “Church,” with
“society,” means bishops, silk-stockings, silver
buckles, broughams, deaconesses, vinegar-faced,
gimlet-eyed scandal-mongers, and the “respect-
ful ” hypocrisy of “ the lower orders.” What with
a belly-heaven beneath and a hospital-paradise
above, this “Church ” between won’t recognise its
identity.

Adelaide (S. A.) Quiz is responsible for a
yarn, of mouldy smell, which may be sum-
marised thus. A pious insolvent offered his
creditors ss. in the £, and consoled them with the
suggestion that the difference would be made up
to them in another world. Thus a matter-of-fact
business son of Belial: —“ Look here, I’m perfectly
willing to take ss. in the <£. but if you expect me'
to go groping all round Slieol after you for the
balance, you’ve got the wrong goat by the tail.
SHAME!
“ Shame 1 ” cries the fat man, if painful disclosure
Should threaten to open their innocent eyes ;
He sits well in front, to prevent an exposure.
And cheers to the echo a parcel of lies.
All things are right, though the case looks suspicious;
There’s nothing corrupt, and no person to blame ;
In a nice state of things the most truly delicious
Is wicked old fat man’s imperative “ Shame ! ”
Sinful old fat man !
Who will kill that man
With poll-axe or poison ; or put him to flame?
O, Fawkner and Batman,
Look down on the fat man !
Were natives like him when to Melbourne you came?
“ Shame I ” cries the fat man when pals are arrested.
And Justice appears to be flashing her sword.
He must have their innocence loudly protested,
Or else precious confidence can’t be restored.
He rests like a blight on the place where his bluster
Has won him a cheap daily-newspaper “ tame ; ”
He shines like a coal-box rubbed up with a duster—
That black-hearted fat man whose gag-word is
“ Shame! ”
Sinful old fat man 1
Who will, &c., &c.
“ Snarae ! ” cries the fat man ; and, whilst he’s permitted
To come to the fore in his armour of brass,
The land where he screams is a land to be pitied,
The Law is a rogue—aye, instead of an ass.
A cute, honest man, playing poker or euchre.
Is skinned by the party who cheats at the game;
And Melbourne is bossed by the fraudulent joker
Who hushes a swindle by crying out “ Shame I "
Sinful old fat man !
Who will kill that man
With poll-axe or poison ; or put him to flame?
O, Fawkner and Batman
Look down on the fat man !
Were people like him when to Melbourne you came?

200,000,000 Dollars Lost.
Seventy-five years ago Thomas L. Walker, then
r mere buy, planted four walnuts by the roadside
opposite Ids father’s house in the eleventh civil
district, near Cedar Bluff, and some ten miles
west of Knoxville. He died ten years ago, and
yet he lived to see four walnut trees grow up to a
measure of four feet in diameter, worth, if cut and
properly seasoned, at least 400 dollars each. Had
he planted 300 walnuts on an adjoining acre of
ground, his heirs, when he died, would have been
120,000 dollars better off. To-day they would
have been 200,000 better off. Had he planted ten
acres they would be worth 200,000,000 dollars.
Had he planted 100 acres, and all the trees had
reached an average size of three feet in diameter,
and there is no reason why they shouldn’t, as the
land is fertile and impregnated with lime, his
heirs, and there are only three living, would be
worth 200,000,000 dollars. If, like old Johnny
Appleseed, who planted thousands of apple trees
in the north-west, he had planted all the worn-out
fields in Tennessee in walnuts, it would be the
richest State in the Union by far. Thousands of
people lose their lives by similiar oversights, they
neglect the important warnings of nature, who
always gives timely warning of the approaching
physical disease. Sometimes it is a* headache,
sometimes neuralgia, toothache, indigestion,
constipation, or what not. Severally and
collectively they indicate that something
is wrong, and wise is he who takes time
by the forelock and has a course of Clements
Tonic, for that is a reliable remedy, as proved by
Mr. B. Simms, hotelkeeper, Charlestown Hotel,
Charlestown, N.S.W., who writes on February
13th, 1893 About two years ago I was a great
sufferer from derangement of the liver. 1 had shoot-
ing pains right through me, also racking head-

Having heard so much about Clements Tonic, I

Before I had finished the third bottle I was re-

same benefit that I have done.—Yours sincerely,
B. Simms, hotelkeeper Charlestown Hotel,
Charlestown, N.S.W.

Word Competition.—No. 3.
Any purchaser of The Bulletin who can
correctly supply the word missing from the para-
graph on the subjoined coupon —which coupon is
to be cut out and forwarded to The Bulletin,
accompanied by one shilling, will be paid the total
amount received in response to this advertisement.
Should there be more than one successful com-
petitor, the total amount received will be divided
amongst such successful competitors pro rata.
Coupons will be received at The Bulletin
Office within six weeks from Saturday, 22nd April,
1893. Should any coupon other than this be used,
the sender will be disqualified, and the amount
added to the prize.
The missing word is placed in a sealed envelope
in the custody of the manager of the Bank of
New Zealand, Sydney, and the contents are
known only to the manager of this paper.
When the prizes are awarded, the winning
word and the names and addresses of the success-
ful competitors will be published in The Bulle-
tin.
Should it happen that no one succeeds in naming
the word, the whole of the money will be added
to that of the succeeding competition.
The sum of one shilling may be forwarded by
Post-office order or stamps of any province; when
the latter are sent, an additional penny to be
added for exchange.

That waggonette which used to draw the
female teachers between the Sydney University
and the training-school will soon lie up for a rest.
One Minister of the Crown never sees it without
putting his hand suddenly against his trousers
pocket, and muttering—“ Retrenchment, retrench-
ment.”
* * *
Mrs. Campbell Praed, in her new story,
“Outlaw and Law-maker,” now b.-ing published
serially in Sydney, makes a “ Lord Horace” sing
this alleged Australian stuff: —
“ The mopokes all are laughing, and the cockatoos are
screamiiir,
And bright among the stringy-barks the parrakeets are
gleaming.’*
The gifted authoress says “ Lord Horace ” sang
with the manner of. one who knew what he was
about. But he evidently didn’t know what the
“mopokes ” were about, for mopokes never laugh,
and either they were out, contrary to custom, in
the daytime, or the naughty cockatoos were
screaming and the vicious parrakeets were gleam-
ing in the otherwise dark Australian night.
Mrs. Campbell Praed’s Queensland memory
gets badly bushed, and the noble Australian editor
is apparently too English to perceive it.
• # *
Justice a’Beckett, of Melbourne, says he
will only inflict a nominal sentence on men who
are convicted of offences against girls of 16, if the
damsels are consenting parties and so developed
physically as to be liable to deceive a man as to
their age. Last week he spoke scathingly from
the bench about “ raising the age of consent.”
# » #
Seven years ago the Deakin - Gillies
Government (Vic.) passed the Shops and Factories
Act, compelling shopkeepers of All sorts to make
every evening in the week a Sabbath. But, not
having courage to enforce the Act, they threw the
onus of shop-closing on the local councils. The
councils gave all traders leave to keep open as
long as they liked if they themselves asked it, and
the Act became a farce. The first blow at its
demolition is the abolition of the elegant J. A.
Levey, the chief inspector, brother of the historic
George Collins.
# # #
A confident young Tasmanian recently
challenged eleven young ladies to a game of
cricket. To his disgust, the girls played a capital
game and beat him badly. An edifying feature of
the match was the bursting of the young man’s—
er —unmentionables, necessitating his retreat to
the dressing-room. He finished the match in a
skirt borrowed from one of his fair opponents.
* * #
A Yankee paper crowds a whole essay on
human nature into a five-line story :
la Dewittsville, N.Y., live Lucinda and Martha Skinner,
74 and 70 years old. Though in the same house, they
haven’t spoken to each other for 47 years. Each has her
own room and does her own work. The original quarrel
was about a stewpan.
What an idea for the believers in a material Hell —
Martha and Lucinda trying for a billion ages to
boil that stewpan over a fire of snowballs, their
salvation to come about the instant the pan boils!
# # *
A strange tale of two undertakers and
only one corpse comes from Western Australia.
Some few weeks ago a man was lying in an almost
hopeless state in Perth Hospital, and a local un-
dertaker, hearing of this, hurried to the patient’s
solicitor and asked for the job of conducting the
burial. The solicitor suggested that the proposal
was premature, and shunted the hireling mourner
out of his office. Late that night, however, the
corpse-planter returned in a cab to announce that
the man had just died and that he was going to
bury him. He was referred to the executor, and
the "executor, who probably didn’t care for being
rushed by undertakers in the dead of night,
flatly declined, and stated that he had given the job
to another practitioner. Thereupon the original
coffin-maker announced that he was going to con-
duct the funeral anyhow, and an unseemly struggle
began for possession of the remains. Both under-
takers rushed round to the hospital and measured
the unresponsive clay. Both came round with
coffins ; but the unauthorised man arrived first,
and thus got the bulge on his enemy. The official
undertaker advertised that he would bury the
deceased at 3 p m., and the unofficial one, to get
ahead of him, advertised, in the same paper, that
he would bury him at 10 in the forenoon. Then
the solicitor interfered. The authorised hearse
and the mourners got to the hospital at 9 a.m.,
and when the other tradesman and his hearse
arrived an hour later the corpse was gone. Also,
his valuable brass-mounted coffin had gone with
it, and he can’t recover it by any means short of
body-snatching and sacrilege.
Why is it, asks a correspondent, that a
parrot picks up swear-words more quickly than
any other forms of speech ? Is it an argument in
favour of original sin, or only a proof that the
human vocabulary consists chiefly of curses?
# * •
A scheme is afoot in N.S.W. for a joint
stock co., capital £50,000, to promote the co-opera-
tive settlement of women for silk-raising. The
promoters are a set of energetic and strong-minded
ladies, but, of course, they have a male god, Mr.
Brady, who, judging from what the associated
ladies write about him in the press, is omniscient,
omnipotent, and, therefore, infallible. There is on
earth no one to equal Mr. Brady as a aericultural
expert. He has locked up in his capacious mind
secrets which it is absolutely impossible for him to
divulge, except for a consideration. Where Brady
got his unique knowledge and experience from,
and whether he ever made a commercial success of
the business no one seems to know. However,
there you are, girls; the silk-worm is our delivered
and Brady is his prophet.

The absurdity of the Melbourne “ Day of
Humiliation ” is shown by the fact that not a
single preacher raised his voice against the Boom,
while it raged. Nay, they rushed after the
boomsters, to lay church memorial stone-, on
which their names were emblazoned —to be erased
when they got to gaol or thereabouts.

Two more victims of “the times”:
Thomas and Nancy Pauley, aged 70 and G 4 years
respectively, out of employment, and unable to
live on charity, join hands, and walk into the sea
at St. Kilda, Melbourne ;
For years they gave the nation their support,
But in return no love the nation shows them—
Wrong rules the land that sits such lives at nought,
And will not pay the honest debt it owes them.

Thomas Dockerell committed suicide at
Ballarat last week, and his brother, Harry, a few
days earlier, both because their savings are under
the debris of a crushed bank. No directors have
felt called upon to hang themselves up to time of
going to press.
# # •
The men baked to death in tin stoke-hole
of H.M.S. Lizard were honoured with a military
funeral. When did the purple bosses of militarism
ever go out of their way to gratify a live stoker?
Despite the glare and glitter of the obsequies, the
gilt-edged magnates were unable to hustle their
hideous blunders into the grave with the poor
victims.

A Melbourne firm has just informed its
town travellers, in a fatherly way, that it will be
obliged if they dispense with the company of their
young lady friends whilst doing the rounds in
future. The average drummer won’t linger in a
shop pushing trade whilst there is pressing busi-
ness to be done in the buggy.

The Katoomba (N.S.W.) Times vain-
gloriously publishes the fact that a burglar suc-
cessfully rifled its cash-box of 355. This is probably
a mean attempt to create a burglar-rush on country
pressmen, and thus make naked their appalling
destitution.
Visiting-magistrate Icely officially looked
in at Lord Howe Island, N.S.W., the other day,
and found the only occupants of the local lock-up
to be some bags of onions. The Lord Howe Island
people didn’t want a lock-up; but the good old
N.S.W. Government forced them to take delivery
of one, which was sent down in sections. Happy
Islet!

Oscar de Satge, a pioneer squatter once
well-known in Queensland Parliament, figures
on an English list of contributors to the Flood
Fund. Oscar’s attempts to adapt himself to bush
life were never more than a half success, because,
while doing as he saw others do, he never relin-
quished his claims to the personal reverence which,
as an employer and a man of titled family, he
conceived to be his due. He forfeited his
chance of election on one occasion by the lan-
guage he addressed to a leading publican who,
in a burst of enthusiasm, slapped him on the
back. De Satge’s repudiation of the familiar
relationship was manifested with such con-
temptuous hauteur that the publican at once
changed sides, and worked day and night for the
enemy. De Satge was the first to take up land
on the Georgina River, and, though a squatter
himself, was an earnest political advocate for the
settlement of the people on the lands of the
interior. He went to England and married there,
long after he had passed middle life, and brought
his bride with him to the wild Di’amantina home,
but they soon returned to England. Oscar will
be Count Oscar when an old man, now beyond 80,
departs.

God Bless the Little Cablegram!
Lord Carington has lent his horse to Captain , to
be used at the opening of the Imperial Institute. —Cable-
gram.
God bless the little cablegram !
That comes across the sea ;
It cheers the boozer o’er his beer,
The beldame o’er her tea ;
It tells how princes, pugs and prigs
Their precious hours employ,
Of Emp’ror Frederick’s stomach-ache,
And Beaty’s baby-boy.
We read : “ The Kaiser Bill is mad.’*
And then there comes the news —
“ ’Tis false that William’s lost his wits
He had no wits to lose.”
Again—“ The Queen fell down the steps
While bent on household cares ;
Her ankle’s sprained—’tis rumoured that
- The Fenians greased the stairs.”
It telleth of the last divorce
In circles high and gay,
And how the old nobilitee
Doth while its time away \ _
It telleth how the Duchess fair
Deceives her darling hub —
What time that haughty nobleman
Is loafing round the pub.
It tells us how the princes live,
And how the poets die,
And how the people cringe and crawl,
And how the statesmen lie ;
It daily serves a dainty dish
Of vice and villainy—
The accursed little cablegram
That comes across the sea.
It tells of Madame Patti’s throat,
And Lottie Collins’ legs,
And how My Lady’s poodle fares,
And how he barks and begs ;
Such sweet tit-bits ! They make one think
How sad the world would be
Without the little cablegram
That comes across the sea.
Queensland.
P. LUFTIG.


Fitzgerald Brothers’ Gigantic Austra-
lian Circus and Menagerie,
The above stupendous institution—the biggest of
the kind ever grown on the soil where the eucalyp-
tus bloometh and the wattle inspireth the local
spring bard —opened on Monday night at Redfern
(Sydney). The audience was tremendous, and
bulged out the huge tent till it was in a dangerous
state of inflation ; also hundreds were turned away
at the gates, and many of them, despite the most
strenuous efforts every night, have never been able
to get in since. The notes of all the banks in New
South Wales were taken at the pay box, and it
was only when the public began to offer preference
shares and eternal fixed deposits in exchange for
seats that the management began to object. They
also declined one old Confederate bond, a large
batch of scrip in the Last Call gold mine, and a
debenture alleged to have been issued by Jorgen
Jorgensen when he ran the Republic of Iceland,
and before he was exiled to Australia. They are
not more particular than the average, but they
felt it was necessary to draw the line somewhere.
In the case of a great many people who are around
at present, the line ought to be drawn around
the neck, and the other end of it fastened to a
tree. But this is a mere side issue, and has no
direct connection with the matter under dis-
cussion.
* • »
One elderly lady who appealed to the generosity
of the firm on the ground that her husband had
been killed in the Golden Treasure mine (Reefton*
M. L.) 18 years ago, was passed in free. So was
another who said that her house had lately been
destroyed by fire, and she thought a sight of the
biggest lion might make her feel better. But
when a tall, gaunt man carrying an angular, blue
umbrella with a sort of pale-green mould upon it,
like a fungus growing in a cellar, wanted to be
put on the free-list because his son was studying
elocution, the brothers again felt it necessary to
draw the line somewhere. Also a firm but cour-
teous negative was given to a person who tried to
get admittance for two in exchange for a ss.
grocery-order by a local benevolent society. They
considered that the spectacle of an enterprising
firm like the Fitzgeralds’ going into Kidman’s to
get ss. worth of groceries in order to realise a
relief ticket might arouse unjustifiable suspicions
as to the solvency of the show. An elderly clergy-
man, however, got in “on the nod” after 25
minutes’ hard argument, in the course of which
he gave a brief history of everything from the
year One to the present day.

Fitzgerald’s Circus is a huge institution which
employs fully 100 men and furnishes work for
over 120 horses. Among
its attractions the
Nubian lions occupy a
high place. In its wild
or natural state the lion
doesn’t amount to much.
It isn’t the animal it is
cracked up to be. It has
very little hair on the top
of its head ; in fact, its
mane is decidedly scraggy,
and it gets bald at a very
early age. It ought to
use Mrs. S. A. Allen’s
world - famed hair - res-
torer. It is of a yellowish
colour, and generally
drags its head and its tail
along dejectedly in the
dust, leaving two big
furrows wherever it goes,
so that after it has
passed by the natives
come and sow oats in
its track. It is always
ip a savage and dejected
state owing to want of
provisions. But when
it gets into a mena-
gerie and has a square
meal regularly, its
hair begins to grow,
and it takes to hold-
ing up its head and
assuming an aspect
of m a g n i fic ence,
and it shakes the
dirt out of the tuft at the end of its tail
and becomes an altogether new lion. It is
an animal of stupendous activity when roused.
Almost everybody who has ever joined with
a lion in forming a procession of two, the lion
being a little way behind and the object of the
procession being to get somewhere in the shortest
possible space of time, has afterwards spoken in
the kindest manner about the rapidity of its
movements, and the business-like manner in which
it didn’t stop on any account to look at the
scenery. Some people have met the lion in a wild
state and seized it by the tail. It is perfectly
easy for an active man to take it by the tail, but
it requires great acumen to let go again success-
fully. The public is invited to inspect the tail of
Fitzgerald Brothers’ big lion, and to consider
itself grasping the animal by that member out on a
great hot plain, with no place of refuge within 10
miles either way, except a solitary palm-tree on
the far horizon, and then think out the above
problem for itself. Anybody who can discover a
reliable way of reaching the palm-tree alive is
hereby invited to write out his scheme in full and
forward it to the principal Railway Commissioner
some day when all his critics have been struck by
lightning on the third Friday in the month.
# « «
But a greater attraction even than the Nubian
lions is the wonderful black-and-white performing
pony, Commodore, a really beautiful animal,
whose brain has developed to such an extent that
fears are sometimes entertained lest lie should
snlit down the back with internal pressure of c<m-
sealed intellect. In the very early days of the
world’s history, about two hundred million years
ago, the horse used to be only an eohippus. That
was before he became a hipparion, and when he
had not even dreamt of being a hippogrif. He
was a small personage about as large as a fox, with
°saws instead of hoofs, was that primitive un-
developed horse . and he travelled with great
velocity over the eocene rocks in order to get
away from the icthyosaurus and the labyrinthodon
pachynathus—which latter animal was a damp
about the size of a cow. That he did get
away successfully, the existence of Commodore
sufficiently proves. In these early days the fastest
men caught the most animals, and the fastest
animals got away from the most men, whereby
the slow men were eaten and the slow animals
starved to death. Commodore’s ancestor, being
the fastest of the lot, kept well ahead of the pro-
cession, and his descendants have done the same
ever since.
# # •
One horse in his time plays many parts, which
statement was originally written by Shakespeare,
who has since been proved not to be Shakespeare
but Bacon, and there are even doubts as to
whether he was either of them. And Commodore
has played more parts than ten average horses put
together. In earlier days, when the firm of Fitz-
gerald Brothers was a small and struggling con-
cern, appealing in a diffident fashion to the back-
blocks intellect, he used to act as almost every-
thing. When the firm deposited its coat and hat
on the grass while it pegged away at getting up
the tent, Oommodore used to sit down beside these
ff arm ents and make sure that they weren’t stolen,
He did this regularly till one day, when Mr. Dan
Fitzgerald laid down an unusually ancient and
ill-fitting hat, and Commodore viewed it with
OSH "TO V\///
""PmUETIN
surprise. Then he deliberately turned it over,
looked at the name of the maker inside, and
promptly resigned his position. His expression
stated distinctly that though he was willing to
make himself useful, still there were some kinds of
hat which a gentlemanly horse could not watch
without injury to his self-respect. Since then he
has positively declined to look after anything
except a bell-topper, and even then it has to be a
new one and carefully brushed.
* # •
Despite this difference of opinion, however,
Commodore continued to act as financial adviser
to the firm, and went round regularly at night to
see that everything was safe. He was always a
sympathetic horse, and many a time in these early
days, when the firm was harassed by a sea of
troubles, he has come and laid his copious silken
ear against the bosom of Mr. Dan Fitzgerald, and
looked up at him with a consoling expression in
his large, bright eye. When Commodore dies —
may that event be many years distant —the
inscription above his last resting-place will be :
“ This is the horse which never went back on a
friend.” And when the caravan passes that way,
it will stop to shed a tear and plant a little simple
flower over his remains. Meanwhile, however,
Commodore doesn’t look at all like dying, and
everybody who has seen his nightly performance
has decided unanimously that it alone is worth
more than the admission money.
• # #
Fitzgeralds’ Circus, like every other great enter-'
prise, had a very small beginning. In its very
earliest stage, when the proprietors were not more
than five years of age, it consisted of a goat which
was led about at the end of a bootlace. There
were no particular points about that goat more
than about any other goat, and he failed to draw,
But his proprietors grew up and persevered. It
was their ambition to create the first great
Australian circus, owned and entirely conducted
by Australians, and after starting with a very
small affair indeed, th6y have at last reached
the gaol of their ambition. Fitzgeralds’ Circus,
though constantly expanding with no visible limit
to its prospective vastness, is already a great con-
cern. Its greatness can only be fully appreciated
when it moves in all the pdmp of the eighteen
waggons, one hundred horses, eighty performers,
and the miscellaneous army of attendants, rouse-
abouts, grooms, cooks, and general assistants, to
say nothing of the proprietors themselves, of Com-
modore and Fanny, who travel as separate insti-'
tutions, the agents who go ahead and gently
approach the depressed and lugubrious local editor,
the literary character who writes the advertise-
ments, the armour-bearers, muleteers, clowns,
heralds, pursuivants, and other functionaries ; also
all the small boys, dogs, and goats for five miles
round who run behind. Then the march of the
circus is like the coming of an army, and Solomon
in all his glory was not a circumstance by com-
parison. Where the circus goes there is a great
cloud of dust rolling in front, and the herbage is
eaten bare behind. Its popularity is great in the
cities of Australia ; but in the barren and lonesome
West it is several degrees more important than a
decent-sized god or an Austrian archduke. It is
the one and only joy which comes to the parched,
sun-baked, silent back-blocks town. Its solitary
rival is hardly worth mentioning in this
connection—is the occasional forlorn dramatic com-
pany which arrives at long intervals with a swag
of faded yellow scenery, and plays a forlorn drama
concerning a persecuted female who was ruined by
the machinations of the person in evening dress
until the unexpected discovery of the tattered will
in the roof of the deserted mansion proves that
the person who is in gaol on a charge of murder is
really half-brother to the dishonest lawyer, where-
upon the principal villain reveals the corpse that
was hidden in the secret passage and falls down
dead leaving three wives to mourn his loss. The
big circus bears up pretty well so far against this
opposition, and hopes to continue doing so,
especially as the clown has got several jokes
freshly upholstered for use during the winter.
Anybody else using the same will be prosecuted.
* « «
The country population having come to regard
the Fitzgerald Brothers’ show as a national
institution which every true and genuine patriot
ought to support, they support it accordingly
with the utmost unanimity. This encouragement
not only appears in the shape of overflowing
houses and eulogistic press notices, but takes
quite a number of diverse forms. When the
itinerant colony is on the march, with somewhere
about (iO or 80 horses trotting contentedly behind,
any loose horse which happens to be grazing in
the vicinity generally joins the procession on his
own account. This sort of thing lays the firm
open to unjust suspicions of having stolen one
bony steed aged 18 years, with a bad spavin and a
partially-defaced brand somewhere near his frayed
mane—that being the general description of the
horse which thus installs himself as the adopted son
of the show. Then the owner’s son, who always
professes to believe that his horse is an animal
that somebody would want to steal, comes in
pursuit, and his beast is disentangled from the
procession, and the firm feels inwardly glad—as
glad as if a near relative had just fallen over a
precipice. Dogs—principally yellow dogs-—testify
to their admiration by attaching themselves to the
company in the same fashion. The Fitzgeralds
calculate that if this evil wasn’t suppressed with a
strong hand they would now—allowing for natural
increase—be the owners of 14,372 dogs, most of
them a cross between the bull-spaniel and that
mysterious kind of depressed terrier which is
always so muddy that no one can tell whether it
is a poodle or a skye - mastiff. Cows also
chip in occasionally, and wander behind
the crowd of animals till it is irnpos-
sible for them to get home in time for milk-
ing. All over the country there is a constant pro-
cession of beasts plodding homewards—rejected
candidates for the circus. Mr. Dan Fitzgerald is
an especial lover of all kinds of animals, and,
recognising this fact, all these waifs and strays
want to lick him softly and lovingly with a great
profusion of red, wet tongue. But for strenuous
outside interference, that gentleman’s clothes
would presently be licked right off him.
* * *
Also, the Fitzgeralds receive occasional en-
couragement in the shape of correspondence. The
following is a sample :
Der surr I ment to go an see your cirkus when you
kame heer but I wasant able to koine as times is very bad
heer just now i w<wd lik if you wood lend me a pound till
g ett hhn a bffleVin gladd.
pleas let me know as soon as posble about the billet also
wood you tell me to deside a bet what time Ernie that
run sekond in the maden plate at in south australya
som time about 1876 or 1879 did the race in don't forget
ffilf have a'job for ayoung man°a°b o ut
22yeersmy oldest sun wood be gladd bein out of graft
ther was a man name Smith left heer to yeers bak he od
me 1 pound 5 if you meet him anywher pleas left me kno
his name was Smith an he had black hare ihat was lame in
the left legg.
The firm has been thinking hard over this letter
for several years, and hasn’t quite decided even
yet whether it was meant to be complimentary or
otherwise. But if it comes across any man whose
hair is lame in the left leg, it intends to collect
that 255. and send it along.
Meanwhile, the big circus hangs out in George-
street, Sydney, opposite the railway-station, and
conveniently adjacent to the Benevolent Asylum,
where it will remain for some time to come.
Judging from the enthusiastic support it is re-
ceiving it might remain there for ever if it felt
that way inclined. It hasn’t suspended, and it
has no desire to reconstruct. Also, if the Govern-
ment desires to publish a proclamation permitting
it to issue notes, the Government will have
to climb over the Fitzgeralds’ bodies to do it. In
spite of a tremendous run, and a panic at the
front gate among the people who are afraid they
won’t get in in time to see Commodore’s great
performance, it continues to meet all claims
promptly. Its clown is the only genuine humorist
at present in Australia, and he intends to continue
so. The other artists are unique in their
respective lines, including the man who swallows
himself, and afterwards spits himself out, the
jugglers, trick riders, gymnasts, the individual
who walks on his ear, and a multitude of others.
The tent is calculated to hold most of the city,
and it is seldom large enough for the business
done. Even the “depression’’ makes no differ-
ence to the Fitzgeralds, and their show goeth on
for ever, and the coin is removed every night in a
truck as usual. Owing to the great increase in
the takings, tenders have lately been called for a
larger truck. Mr. Tom Fitzgerald attends to the
business end of the show as usual, while Mr. Dan
looks after the interior arrangements. Also, Air.
Lyons is Press representative as heretofore. *

More Australian Testimony.
THE SECRETS OF SUCCESS.
Mr. J. B. Warbrick, or “Joe Warbrick,” as he
is familiarly known, was captain of the New
Zealand Native Football Team (better known as
“The Maoris”), which visited the Old Country
and gave such a good account of itself during the
season there of 1888-89. This team firmly believed
that St. Jacobs Oil is one of the secrets of success
in football, and kept itself in prime condition by a
free use of it. The team was so unanimous in its
verdict that the matter is worthy of special men-
tion. Mr. Warbrick, in conversation with one of
the representatives of the Chas. A. Vogeler Co.,
in Sydney, after their return, stated: “I have
much pleasure, in testifying to the efficacy of St.
Jacobs Oil. I have used it constantly since my
first visit to New South Wales in 1884, having
then injured my knee in our first match ; and dur-
ing our tour in Great Britain we have used noth-
ing else, and the New Zealand Native Football
Team all speak in the highest terms of its
qualities. In fact, so much did we think of it
that we hardly ever travelled without a case
AN ENDORSEMENT.
Mr. J. R. Scott, the successful proprietor of the
leading hotel in Gisborne, N.Z.—the Masonic
Hotel —and the able manager of the above-men-
tioned New Zealand football team on their tour
through Great Britain, endorses the above by
letter, in which he states ; “ I have much pleasure
in testifying to the wonderful properties of St.
Jacobs Oil. While there (Great Britain) I got for
the team’s use nearly a dozen different embroca-
tions, but none seemed to do the men the same
amount of good as St. Jacobs Oil. In fact, we
never considered our baggage complete without a
case of it.”

SPORTING NOTIONS.
“Billy” Murdoch rages at his exclusion from
the Australian Eleven. Public opinion is with Victor
Cohen, as Murdoch, who now wears tan boots and tucks
his trousers up, can scarcely be looked upon as a repre-
sentative Australian.
# * *
At a recent Sunday camp down Middle
Harbour, Sydney, where chops were scarce and newly-
anived Britishers plentiful, a hungry young Australian
Collected a few dozen wood-grubs*, with which the chops
were garnished when served. The Britishers declined
with thanks, and picknicked bn bread and jam. Yet
wood-grubs are not bad eating. Roasted pop-corn and
toasted wood-grubs are hardly to be distinguished by
taste.
* * *
A N.S.W. country paper states that ex-cham-
pion Edward Trickett (now in Sydney) is in need of
pecuniary help, and that a subscription-list has been
started for him.
• • •
Lacrosse, the Canadian game, is growing in
favour in S.A. There are already seven metropolitan
clubs, and several in the country; The game is supported
by the men who are driven from football, with its
“shady ” associations. No game is pure where the flies
the bookmakers—swarm round the carcase.
