Boys ” at Lord’s last week, played through the whole of
the ’92 season in England without getting a bigger score
than SC. His average for 25 innings was only 12 runs.
* # *
Baird, deceasedj paid his retaining-fee of £5OOO
to jockey Watts in advance. The Duke of Portland was
to give tIOOO a year for second call on the artist, but now
that Baird has gone, the Duke, so the English Jockey
Club decides, is entitled to step into the dead man’s shoes
and be “ first master ” for the season without paying any-
thing at all! The question that arises is-supposing Baird
hadn’t paid in advance, would the Duke of Portland have
taken the vacant place on payment of £lOOO, or would
Watts be free to put himself up to auction again ? How-
ever, Baird unintentionally bequeathed his investment to
Portland, and his death leaves Watts with more spare
mounts to dispose of. Happy jockey !
* * *
Sport runs in the blood. W. J. Gormly, the
crack N.S.W. swimmer, is a son of Gormly, M.L.A., who
rode Camel in the historic ten-mile race at Wagga a
quarter of a century ago, a couple of years before the
crack swimmer’s birth.
» • •
Billy M'Carthy defeated La Blanche (“ The
Marine ”) in the 16th round at New Orleans, U.S., on
Thursday last, aftef a savage fight. La Blanche, the
erstwhile conqueror of the famous “ Nonpariel ” Demp-
sey, must have faded considerably. It is hardly likely
that M‘Carthy has “ come on ” to any great extent.
# * *
Griffo, the champion Australian featherweight,
duly departed for America by the Alameda. He may be
a different man away from the noxious gang by whom he
has been shepherded in Sydney, but it is only fair to let
the Yankees know that Griffo is scarcely a fair represent-
ation of the Australian boxer-outside the ring. The
writer cannot sum up the departing pug better for the
Americans than by remarking that Griffo “ on the burst
—his chronic state-is a miniature edition of Jonnel
Sullivan, without any of the redeeming qualities said to
be possessed by the Yankee in his sober moments.
# * *
A London paper tells this yarn re John
Roberts, the billiardist: —“J. R was playing a client
giving him long points in fifty. The amateur reached 49
to John Roberts's love. Red was on the spot. Roberts s
ball down. The other gave a miss in baulk. Young
John—he was young then—went to the spot end of the
table and took down the clock, and, playing from baulk,
forced his own ball and the red off. In those days you
were not penalised for sending a ball off the table, as you
are now. His opponent gave another miss in baulk,
Roberts repeated his stroke, and so they went on till the
game was 49 all. The other was obliged to play at the
red and left a score on which gave Roberts game.”
« • •
One day last week, “The Council of Churches ”
had the cheek to petition the Victorian Cabinet to pro-
claim a public half-holiday, “ to he observed as a time of
humiliation and prayer." The committee of a certain
pony-racing club, in anticipation of the petition being
granted, and to cater for the non-humiliating crowd,
promptly framed a scratch race meeting for the same
afternoon. The events comprising the proposed pro-
gramme were the Supplication Stakes, the Humiliation
Handicap, the Prayerful Plate, the Sinners Selling Race,
the Saints’ Steeple and the Collection Plate. No enter-
prise in Victoria, did you say ?
* * *
Tommy Corrigan, the steeplechase-rider, one
day last summer occupied the same railway-compartment
as a tough-looking countryman. The yokel, without
warning, proceeded to take off his boot, much to Tommy s
disgust. “Mv foot’s asleep,” explained the traveller on
observing Tommy’s horrified expression. “‘Asleep, did
ye sa3 r ? ” remarked Thomas. “Egad, I’d have sworn it
was dead ! ”
* * *
Priceless gem from Sydney D.T. : “ ‘ Picaninny
is the name bestowed on the bay gelding filly by Invermay
from Morceau.” The career of this comprehensive
animal will be watched with interest by all true lovers of
sport. #
The Australian Eleven’s Bristol match aerainst
Gloucestershire was a spoilt walk-over, the Australians
holding up the wickets for over a day and a-half, and
only consenting to retire after knocking up 503—G.
Giffen, 180: Trott, 68 ; Graham, 59; Lyons, 48 ; Comng-
ham, 43; Bannerman, 39: M‘Leod, 36. G'ff en and
Graham were both “stumped.” The Gloucester-
Shire men only managed to put together 41 runs in
their first innings, and were enabled by rainy weather
to cry a go. George Giffen took seven wickets
for 11 runs ; Trumhle, 2 for 14 ; Coningham 1 for 12. In
the match at Lord’s, between the Australians and the
M.C.C. and Ground, the Englishmen took first innings
and rattled up 424 runs at the rate of about 100 per hour
—Flowers, 130; Marchant. 103; Stoddart, 51; Wilson
and Attewell, 31 apiece ; Gunn, 28 ; VV. G. Grace, 13.
The Australians duly made 243 in their first innings, but
saved their bacon with 347 in the second innings, the
match being drawn (unconcluded) with the Englishmen
having 14 runs to make and two wickets to fall—Stoddart,
45 ; VV. G. Grace, 33 ; Attewell, 26. Australian scorers :
Lyons, 0 and 137 ; Bannerman, 10 and 75 ; Gregory, 41
and 31; Graham, 29 and 24; Coningham, 40 and 0;
Bruce, 37 ; Trott, 29. The Australians, on Monday, were
matched against Yorkshire, at Sheffield ; other immediate
fixtures—2sth, Lancashire, at Manchester; 29th, Surrey
Oval; June Ist, Oxford University. Record Against
one defeat, one win and two creditable “draws.”

Pugilist Hall is said to have struck Slavin
twice, with intent to exasperate him. But Slavin only
said—“ You get backers, and I’ll give you a chance in
the ring. I don’t fight for amusement;”
Premier Dibbs is not the first mail whom
Mr. Tom Slattery has saved from cross-examina-
tion in the Insolvency Court witness-box.
The “ Oook” papers, forming “The Histo-
rical Records of N.5.W.,” second volume, will be
published shortly under the nominal editorship of
Mr, F. W. Bladen, Clerk of Records in the Govt.
Printing Office. As the Government has abolished
the History Board, and has not filled the vacant
historiographership, it is presumed that the costly
literary experiment entered upon byParkes peters
out.
At the recent type-setting contest at Mel-
bourne Rotunda, the “copy” given out was por-
tion of an essay on “ the Advantages of Christian
Unity, and the Evils of Sectarianism.” The con-
test was won by a Standard “ whip.” This sort
of copy would be little short of sudden death to
the average compositor, but a man accustomed to
the matter usually contained in the daily papers,
can set anything, without growing dispirited;
Angus & Robertson, of Castlereagh-street*
Sydney, advertise in another column, offering
double prices for back numbers of The Bulletin
of dates specified 5 besides promising special in-
ducements for old pamphlets, letters, MSS., &c.,
of historial Australasian interest.
Everyone is retrenching all round just
now, but there is still plenty of cash in circulation
for a judicious investment, so comes to meet the
times “The Columbian Land Distribution,” a
scheme whereby a speculator to a small amount
stands the chance of acquiring a comfortable
competency. The management is in the hands of
men of standing, who court enquiry as to
the hona-fides of the undertaking. Further par-
ticulars will be found in our advertising columns.
“ The Advertiser’s ABO” for 1893, pub-
lished by T. B. Browne, 161 and 163 Queen
Victoria-street, London, E.C., and 353 and 355
Canal-street, New York, is a complete and
extremely useful compilation, showing the world’s
newspapers and their official scales of advertising
charges. An intelligible classification of papers
into their various class and trade divisions
occupies some preliminary space in the work,
and is followed by “A Chronological Survey of
the History of the Newspaper Press,” which
shows that, as early as 1526, news-sheets were
published in certain German and Austrian towns.
The first English newspaper was the Mercurie,
in 1588, “published by authoritie for the preven-
tion of false reports.” A “Journalistic Obituary
for ’92” is succeeded by an article on “The
Year’s Changes.” The section devoted to Aus-
tralasia is made concisely valuable to the ad-
vertising business-man in that portion set
apart for literary description. A few slight
indications of, a twisted idea of the relative im-
portance of Australasian newspapers are insepar-
able inaccuracies from a distant viewpoint, and
on all the more solid and important business
details, the “Advertiser’s A B C”isa valuable
and reliable compendium, and can safely be
recommended to advertisers as an authority upon
“circulating mediums.”

the front door open.

Jack Keyes’ Wife: A Tale of Equatorial
Polynesia.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Years ago, in the days when the “ highly
irregular proceedings ” of the brig Carl and
Other Britisli ships engaged in the trade
which The Times has labelled “absolutely
above reproach,” attracted some attention from
the Britisli Government towards the doings of the
gentlemanly scoundrels engaged therein, the
people of Sydney used to talk proudly of the fleet
6f gunboats whifch, constructed by the late Mr.
Cuthbert, of Miller’s Point, wete going to “ patrol
the various recruiting grounds of the Fijian and
Queensland planters and place the labour-traffic
under the most rigid supervision.” The remark
quoted above was then, as it is now, quite a hack-
neyed one, much Used by the gallant officers who
donlmanded the one-gun-one-rocket-tube craft
afore-mentioned. Likewise, the “ highly irregular
proceedings ” wete a rtaval synonym fot some of
the bloodiest slaving outrages ever done by man,
but h'oweVer,- neVer came to light beyond
being alluded to’ as' “unreliable and unauthenti-
fcated statements by discharged and drunken sea-
meri'” # * #
The Australian slave-suppressing vessels
Were not a success. Irt the first place, they couldn’t
sail any faster than a mud-dredge; Poor Bob
Randolph, of the Gilbert and Kingsmill Groups,
employed as pilot and interpreter on board, once
remarked to the officer commanding one of these
wonderful tubs that for four days had been
thrashing her way against the S.E. trades in a
heroic endeaVour to get inside Tarawa lagoon,
distant ten miles (and couldn’t do it), that “these
here schooners ought to be rigged as fore-and-
afters and called 4 four-and-halfters for, I’ll be
hanged if this thing can do more than knots,
even in half a gale of wind, all sail set and a
smooth sea;” But if the“fodr-and-ha]fters,”as they
Were thenceforth designated in the Western Pacific,
were useless in regard to the villainies and
slaughter of the labour trade, there was one
instance in which one of the schooners and her
captain did some good by avenging as cruel a
murder as was ever perpetrated in equatorial
Oceania.
# « #
Old Jack Keyes was a trader on the island
of Apiang, one of the group recently annexed by
the Royalist. He was very old, very quiet in his
manner, and about the last kind of man one would
expect to see located as a trader among the excit-
able, intractable native race that peoples the Line
Islands. His tellow-trader, Bob Randolph, a
man of tremendous nerve and resolution, only
maintained his prestige among the Apiang natives
by the wonderful control he had learnt to exercise
over a naturally fiery temper and by taking care,
when knocking down any especially insulting
native 44 buck,” never to draw blood, and always
to laugh. And the people of Apiang thought
much Of Te Matan Bob, as much as the inhabi-
tants of the w’hole group —from Arorai in the
south to Makin in the north—do to this day of
quiet, spectacled Bob Corrie, of wild Maiana, who
can twist them round his little finger without an
angry word. Perhaps poor Keyes, being a
notoriously inoffensive man, might have died a
natural death in due time, but for one fatal
mistake he made ; and that was in bringing a
young white wife to the island.
* * •
A white woman was a rarity in the Line
Islands. Certainly, the Boston mission ship,
Morning Star, in trying to establish the 44 Gospel
according to Bosting—no lie or dollars, no mis-
sn’ry,” as Jim Garstang, of Drummond’s Island,
used to observe, had carried a lady soul-saver of
somewhat matured charms, but her advent into
the Apiang moniap or town hall, carrying an
abnormally large white umbrella, had had the
effect of scaring the assembled councillors away
across to the weather-side of the narrow island,
whence none returned Until the terrifying
apparition had gone back to the ship. But this
white Woman was different, and the Apiang
native, like all the rest of the world, is susceptible
to female charms $ and her appearance at the
doorway of the old trader’s house was ever hailed
with an excited chorus of 14 Te boom te matan! Te
boom te inat&n ! ” (The whiteman’s wife.) But
none were rude or offensive to her, although the
young men especially were by no means chary of
insulting the old man.
* # *
One of these young men was unnecessarily
intrusive. He would enter the trader’s houseon any
available pretext, and the old fellow then noticed
that he would let his savage eyes rest upon his
wife’s figure in a way there was no mistaking.
Not daring to tackle the brawny savage, whose
chest, arms, and back were one mass of corruga-
tions resulting from wounds inflicted by sharks’
teeth, spears, and swords in many encounters, old
Jack verbally intimated to his visitor to 44 get.”
The savage, with sullen hate gleaming from his
cruel eyes, that looked out from the mat of coarse,
black hair, which, cut away in a fringe over his
forehead, fell upon his shoulders, rose slowly and
went out.
* * *
Early next morning old Keyes was going
over to Randolph’s house, probably to speak of
the occurrence of the previous day, when his wife
called him and said that someone was at the door
waiting to buy tobacco. 44 What have you to
sell?” called out the old man. 44 Te moe motu”
(young drinking-cocoanuts) was the answer, and
the old man, not recognising the voice as that of
his visitor of the day before, went unsuspectingly
to take them from the native’s hand, when the
latter, placing a horse-pistol to the trader’s heart,
shot him dead, with the savage exclamation:
44 Now your wife is mine !”
# # *
The poor woman fled to Bob Randolph for
safety, and, dreading to remain on the island,
went away in a schooner to her home in Maori-
land. Nearly a year passed, and then a man-of-
war came and endeavoured to capture the
murderer, but in vain, for the captain would not
use force, and 44 talk ” the natives only laughed at.
So the ship steamed away ; and then the natives
began to threaten Randolph, and talk meaningly
to each other about the store being full of tc pakca
and te rom (tobacco and gin). A long, uneasy six
months passed, and then the little “four-and-
halfter” Reward, Commander Pugh, ran into
Apiang lagoon, and the naval officer told Randolph
he had come to get the man and try him for the
murder.
# # •
He first warped his vessel in as near as
possible to the crowded village, and moored her
with due regard to the effectiveness of his big gun.
Then, with Randolph as interpreter, negotiations
commenced.
The old men were saucy; the young men wanted
a fight and demanded one. Randolph did his
part well. He pointed out to the old men that
unless they gave the man up the long gun on the
ship Would destroy every house and canoe on the
island, even if no one were killed. That meant
much to them, whereas one man’s life was but
little. But, first, the natives tried canning. One
and then another wretched slave Was caught and
bound and taken off to the naval officer as the'
murderer,- only to be scornfully rejected by Ran-*
dolph and the captain. Then the officer’s patience
was exhausted. If the man who murdered KeyeS
was not Surrendered in an hour he would open
fire; and also hang some of the chiefs then de-
tained on board as hostages.
Randolph’s gloomy face quickened their fears,
This Captain could hot either be frightened or
fooled. In half-an-hour, the, slayer of the trader
was on board. The old men admitted their attempt
at deception, but pleaded that the murderer
was a man of influence, and they would rather the
two others were hanged than this one. The trial
was just and fair, but short, and then Randolph
urged the captain to have the man executed on
shore by being shot. It would impress them mote
than hanging him on board. And hanging they
regarded as a silly way of killing a man.
* 4 •
The captain had no relish for work of this
nature, and when Randolph told him that the
natives had consented to execute the prisoner in
his (Randolph’s) presence and the captain’s pre-
sence also if necessary, he, no doubt, felt glad.
Bob Randolph then became M.C. and gave his
instructions to the Old men. The whole village
assembled ifi front of Randolph’s to see the show,
An old carronade lying in the corner of the copra
house was dragged Out, cleaned,- and loaded with
a heavy blank charge. Then the prisoner, sullen
and defiant to the last, but wondering at the car-
ronade, was lashed with his back to the muzzle,
and, at a signal from one of the old men, a fire-
stick was applied to the gun. A roar, a rush of
fragments through the air, and all was finished.
Bob Randolph’s fox-ter-rier was the only one that
seemed to trouble about making any search for
the remnants of the body. Half-an-hour after-
wards he came in ahd deposited a gory lump of
horror sit Bob’s feet.
# * *
This act of retribution raised a howl of
indignation in England. Pugh was vilified and
his dismissal from the navy demanded in Parlia-
ment. However, he was made a post-captain long
ago, and is always spoken of in the Line Islands
as “the best naval officer we ever knew.”
LOUIS B.

“ Truth is stranger than fiction.” In none
of his sea-stories has Clark Russell over conceived
such a situation as that of the missing boat of the
“ Templemore : ” five days alongside an iceberg
with but 81bs. of biscuits amongst 24, and the
captain’s wife taking her turn at the oar with the
rest.
It is pointed out that Europe, in 1892,
produced 2,560,800,000 gal. wine, and Algeria
72 200,000, while the Australian yields were—Vic-
toria, 1,554,130; South Australia, 801,835; N.S.
Wales, 913,107 ; Queensland, 168,526 ; West Aus-
tralia, ’166,202. The other day a man who ought
to be an agricultural authority said that he would
advise any man with a little capital in search of a
living to get land in the Hunter (N.S.W.) district
and grow vines.

Ode to Nothing.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Spirit of night and day enwrapped in a mystio
mist,
Light of the lights that rise telling cf darkness
nigh,
Take from the earth its earth —the sin by the
serpent hiss’t,
Withering life to death, dooming the born to
die,
Blot from the book of Time the wrath of the
years gone by.
Sorrows of land and sea, sadding the sea and land,
Vanish in sighs and find rest in the bourn of
care;
Shadows of fears unshaped shall scatter at joy’s
command,
Like rustling red-brown leaves on the breath of
a cold despair,
And the rhyme of the rhymester gleams through
a glamour of sense not there.
Night bringeth dawn of day, for day is the end of
night;
Writ in the book of Time is the wrath of the
bygone years;
The sin of the Serpent lives in the bosom of vain
delight,
All the hope of the world lies crushed by a
burden of secret tears,
And the rhyme of the rhymester rings in a won-
dering reader’s ears.
Earth that is merely earth, sea that is more than
salt,
Signs of a worldly world, fashioned from air and
cloud, .
Ye are as bones decayed to dust in a goblins
vault,
Echoing cries unheard of the sleeper who sleeps
aloud.
Life is a pang of pain, and the end of it all—a
shroud.
Song of the writhing worm sung to a mortal
host—
Strain of the stricken soul passioned with grief
sublime!
Naught is the dimplei babe but the germ of a
grizzly ghost,
The taint" of the time that was attainting the
end of time.
And the rot of mankind must bo as the rot of
the rhymester’s rhyme.
Melb.
E.F.
A Pair of Runaways.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Lady Whitaker was a comely young
woman. As is sometimes the case, she was
married to an old man. It is an open question
how far the husband has a right to be selfish iff
these instances. The pair were botn aver-’e, and
Nature should have prevented the union; but that
goddess didnlt. i
True, despite her love fbr atidther, she had
snapped at title ahd Wealth; Biit, theh, She knew
what poVerty was—it had driveil her own sistei
into the street. Tq-day, riorte dare deny the
wisdom o! her choice. So do our ideas progress.
The noble lord suffered tetribly from gout,
trout, like death, helps to balance things a little;
It seldom attacks those vVhd sweat fteely, and
those who starve liberally.
However, the malady contributed greatly td
Lady W.’s comfort. She was unmolested in her
frequent sallies. She was a superb horsewoman.
To see her in the saddle, that was indeed unison.
On a certain Tuesday, his lordship swore at her.
She gave a port twist of defiance as she left the
room. Securing the phaeton, she was soon hying
along the high-road, when she quite unexpectedly
met the head-groom. Humour said that he
resembled her burst-up early lover. The horses
Were stopped, the lady descended, and the couple
sauntered arm-in-arm across the fields tb gathet 1
wild flowers. . i.
The silent pressure, the gentlb smbbzmg of the
moustache, and the noiseless peck, peck, of
feminine lips are well known to some of us. ibis
last item reminds trie of the keri bpring for sheep’s
kidney-fat. The Symptoms are signs of the swap :
ping of magnetisms. What a pity sighs and
squeezes cannot be stacked ill bundles !
* • •
The horses, losing patience, Suddenly
bolted. The groom would have pm>ued; but the
lady held him firmly j “Never mind the horses,”
she said, eloquently. And so these reasoning
beings lingered, while time fled and horses sped.
* # *
The careering animals knocked down a
man and broke his accursed leg. I say accursed,
because the accident was the pivot of the ex-
posure. It is curious how sin is so often dis*
covered, while virtue remains intact. The latter
makes a fine overcoat for spies.
Some presents were burned, and a young fellow
made for Australia.
# * *
The man who thus became a digger died
suddenly in my arms in a smithy last week. After
all, woman is a grand institution.
Moral: Prize out the bump of curiosity trom
the heads of your children with a cold-chisel.
Maoriland.
POLYPE.


The Wild-Cat Column.
The City of Melbourne Bank, which collapsed
last Wednesday, is an institution which ought not to be
allowed to reconstruct; it should rather he abolished by
Act of Parliament. Its whole biography consists of one
sentence ; it had four-and-a-lialf millions in deposits, of
which three-and-a-quarter millions were British capital.
The City of Melbourne Bank was a mere rat-trap for
British money, and a pawnbroking institution tor lending
out the same in Australia at massive interest. And, as
Australia is already cursed with far too much foreign
capital, a bank which devotes itself entirely to this kind
of business is a disease—and a pretty bad one at that.
The City of Melbourne did so little Australian business
that, in its local capacity, it was hardly worth calling a
bank. It had only five branches in Australia, and it had
five agencies in Britain for the purpose of gathering in
deposits ; therefore, like a shark, it was pretty nearly all
mouth. Also; as the result showed, it was a mouth with
a spiked tongue, the spikes all pointing inwards, so that
it only worked one way.
* * *
The constant cry of the daily press that the
Australian banks issue notes only on the security of a
gold reserve, and are, therefore, far more sound than a
State Bank which, it is falsely assumed, would issue notes
with no gold reserve, is getting wearisome. Also, as it is
about time to point out, there is nothing in it. The
banks don’t issue notes on any gold-reserve; they
only do it On the security of their own unmiti-
gated gall. According to latest returns, the New
South Wales banks had £1,400,000 worth of notes
in circulation, and nearly £10,000,000 of deposits at
call, and their total coin reserve against both these
liabilities was less than £6,000,000. When a rush comes,
it is always a rush of depositors, and when they have got
out their money, or even half of it, the alleged note
Reserve has vanished into thin air. The reserve, in fact,
is a deficiency of about £3,000,000, and if there were no
call deposits and no gold leserve at all the noteholders
would be just £3,000,000 more secure than they are at
present.
* * *
The new issue of notes by the Bank of New
South Wales is a fearsome production—from a police-
man’s point of view. When N.S.W. Premier Dibbs’
famous Bank Issue Bill was made law the B.N.S.W. had
a fresh consignment of paper money to arrive from
England, but being unable to wait for it the management
instructed a firm of Sydney printers to grind out a lot of
currency on the instant. The firm had to do the work in
red-hot haste, and consequently the new note is a
feeble affair with a splodge of yellow on it—a thing
which looks as if it had been lithographed at a gallop.
The Bulletin knows several talented French gentle-
men who arrived in Australia viii New Caledonia, who
could turn out, inside of a week, a note altogether undis-
tinguishable from the new promise-to-pay, and as one at
least of this crowd looks unusually affluent just now* it
seems quite possible that he is already on the job. There
are, we fear, very happy times ahead for the bogus-note
gentlemen. Since paper has been declared legal tender it
keeps in circulation much longer than it used to do, and
many of the bogus notes will never he suspected until
the bank gives up its gold, and by that time the artist will
be far, far away. If the Treasury has to pay up under its
guarantee, it will probably be a very much surprised
department when the paper begins to roll in, and as it
has no special knowledge of bank notes it will, as likely as
not, pay the bogus ones quite cheerfully until their un-
natural abundance begins to arouse its suspicions and
make its well-oiled hair stand on end. Meanwhile, the
issue of some new kind of Treasury bill to relieve de-
positors on current account is under consideration, so that
N»S. Wales will have all kinds of money soon—except
coin—and the man who takes a threepenny omnibus-ride
will pay his fare with an inscribed deposit certificate, and
get back as change one preference share, an inconvertible
bank-note, a Treasury bill, and the ’bus co.’s tickets for
the small change, said tickets being convertible into
groceries at Kidman’s by special arrangement.
* # #
Speaking of paper money, The Bulletin is
reminded that a certain bankruptcy assignee has a room
wherein are hung framed specimens of the many elaborate
notes formerly issued by Thomas Smith Richardson,
banker, of Sydney, the said Thomas Smith R. being now
in gaol. Underneath them are pasted eulogistic notices
from nearly all the daily papers in Sydney, setting forth
by innuendo the respectability of the same blastiferous
swindle, and the reliability of the paper in question. None
of these papers had the slightest evidence of Thomas Smith
Richardson’s solidity except his own bald assertion ; but he
was a good advertiser, and that apparently was enough.
Richardson, among his other achievements, succeeded in
trading £3OOO worth of his waste-paper on a Cumberland
fruit-grower, and the old man, with a profound and child-
like faith in bank-notes of any kind, kept them carefully
in a bag. But, at last, his son got into dreadful
trouble, and the kindly patriarch gave him the bag
of Smith Richardson’s money to save him from destruc-
tion. For days after that a distracted young man humped
the whole lot around the city trying in vain to get a bid
atOOpercent. discount, and at last betook somewhere
about £6O for the lot. This sort of thing inspires an
awful lot of faith in N.S.W. paper currency, yet there is
no definite law against it, and nobody seems inclined to
make one. The only thing that is being made is more
paper. This ceaseless grinding out of more paper-money
is about the only industry established in N.S.W. up to
date by Dibbs’ half-hearted revenue tariff.
* # •
Looking back from the standpoint of to-day,
the public feels a certain dull admiration at the “ front ”
of the Commercial Bank of Australia. Its magnates
evidently realised that a general collapse was coming,
and, therefore, they collapsed first, and got their recon-
struction through before the panic began, and the
yearning to kill every banker in the land took possession
of the popular intelligence. The terms they imposed on
their creditors were about the grandest example of gall
and wormwood on record, and the board which pro-
posed such terms now would have to run for
its life. And they got their institution re-
opened in time to gather up the ruins of their
(alien competitors, and to put on airs of superiority as
they did it. When the Commercial of Australia suspended.
Manager Dibbs of that superior institution, the Commer-
cial of Sydney, loudly expressed a hope that when they
did start again it would be under another title, as the
similarity of names was very—haw!—inconvenient. This
was the pride that goeth before a big smash. Now the
Commercial of Sydney is shut up, and making slow
progress with its reconstruction, and Manager Turner of
the other Commercial no doubt -ays that he hopes Dibbs’
bank will begin again under a new design ition, so as to—
haw [—prevent mistakes. They ail feel like that. Every
time a weak bank shuts up the manager of the alleged
strong bank across the road would come and dance a war-
dance on his fallen rival’s doorstep only his dignity stands
in the way.
• • •
According to published statements, it would
appear that the Queensland National Bank has been more
or less on the down grade since 1880. In that year it
reached the summit of its glory and the net profits were
announced to lie £133,000. In 1890, they were £lOl,OOO ;
in 1891, they got down to £77,916 ; and in 1892 they stood
at £78,373. The capital was £BOO,OOO, and as a 10 per
cent, dividend, by any known proci ss of arithmetic, takes
£BO,OOO, it would seem as if the Q.N. had, for the last two
years, been paying away more than it earned.
# * *
After the preliminary newspaper paragraphs
which announced that Pliant O’Loghlen had carefully
abstained from reading the press leports of the Mer-
cantile case, and similar bluff, the susceptible Irishman’s
decision was a foregone conclusion.
« • •
The Melb. “City Newspaper C 0.,” which some-
how sprang from the ashes of Matthew Davies and Bal-
four’s wretched Daily Telegraph and which at present
is running the local ’Eralb, gave itself away recklessly
when the Mercantile case was “abindoned.” “The
commercial reputation of the city is surely purged of a
great scandal by this finding,” piped the ’Eralb, previous
to piping a little more about “ the cost to the country of
a criminal prosecution.” But next morning the Age even
said—" The misfortune is that the mischief does not end
with this kind of a discharge of the alleged culprits. It
has gone abroad to the public that hanking methods
which are shocking to common sense are quite permissible
under our law.” And everybody swore at large, with the
exception of the 'Eralb, which considered the city had
been “purged.”
# # #
It is more than a year since The Bulletin
observed that “confidence” would never “ be restored ”
until a gaol-wall was erected around Melbourne city.
Picture the wives and daughters from Toorak and St.
Kilda casting plugs of tobacco into the spacious exercise-
yard I Imagine the tone of the letters attached to the
plugs—“ Dearest James, —Yesterday morning I had to
discharge one of the female menials for insolence. She
said that her ‘ chap ’ would be ‘ out ’ two years before
‘mine.’—Yours, in tears and luxury, Maubie Paubib.”
* « «
Matthew Davies “preferred not to say any-
thing ” about the O’Loghlen nolle prosequi. Why didn’t
he say “Hallelujah!” and let the admiring 'Eralb
reporter chip in with “ Amen ?”
* * *
The unsatisfactory Ainslie, who couldn’t be
induced by the Crown Prosecutors to say anything about
the Mercantile Bank, and was kept in the background
along with Graham Berry, did say something concerning
O’Loghlen’s refusal to prosecute. He said: “Further
proceedings would have been very unfair. All they could
have resulted in would have been more expense for Mr.
Millidge and less receipts for Sir Matthew’s creditors.”
How long, O Lord ! are “ opinions ” of this kind to be
set up in large type by the man who prints for a syndicate
of hushers-up?
* * *
Most of the re-constructing banks propose to
pay all their creditors with shares or long-dated paper,
except note-holders, the Government (which has a prior
claim), and “ other preference creditors.” Who are the
“ other preference creditors ? ”
* * *
Some of the Sydney newspapers, even after the
suspension of the bank, quoted the shares of the Com-
mercial of Sydney, at “sellers 80’’—sales having been
effected with difficulty at 45.
* # *
Item of batik news : “ The banks of Melbourne,
prior to the recent troubles, held about £13,000,000 in
coin, gold, and consols. It is estimated that they now
hold £10,500,000.” So that a pull of £2,500,000 on the
alleged coin overturned the show.
# • •
One of the causes given for the big insolvency of
Mr. Yencken, of Melbourne, is “ depreciation in the
value Of hU freehold residence.” It is difficult to see how
a gentleman can get into debt through a reduction in the
rent; which he, as tenant, pays to himself, as landlord.
At this rate a sudden fall in the price of diamonds might
bankrupt the husband of a lady who wore a £lO,OOO neck*
lace day and night.
* * *
“ Does having been convicted of a felony dis-
qualify for a seat on a bank directorate ?” was a question
asked at a recent Sydney reconstruction meeting. The
voice was a gentle one, and its owner a mild, non-mili-
tant-looking greyheaded person in black, but it made
one of the nominated reconstructionists as red in the face
as if he had been asked to publicly exhibit the securi-
ties for his overdraft.
# * •
The Colonial Bank of Australasia “re-con-
structed ” even its own dividend. The 8 per cent, alleged
profit was payable to shareholders on and after a certain
momentous Friday, but though a few of the city share-
holders turned up promptly and got their cash, very few
of the country ones were on the spot. From Monday to
Friday inclusive were holidays, and the institution kept
closed. On Saturday it suspended, atid the disappointed
stockholder who arrived in a frenzy at the big front door
was “left,” like the public in general. The bank that
falls down dead in the very act of paying a dividend is
obviously an institution with heart-disease.
* » •
A bank’s “ reserve fund ’* is one of those things
Which religiously disappear when called for, and the
“contingency account” seems to have no existence in
fact when contingencies require to be met. Melbourne
has had recent experience of banks professing to hold
large reserve funds until they closed their doors, where-
upon the liquidators Came in and hunted for these
mythical resources in Vain.
• • •
The “confidence” doctrine is like a letter full
of the deepest sympathy for a good cause, but containing
no cash. Men with abundance of money have no confi-
dence. Those with heaps of confidence have no money.
* * *
Possibly the Fat Man who has successfully
warded off land and property taxes and worked the
expenditure of millions in unprofitable railways now
appreciates the result of his handiwork.
• # #
“Tame Cat” writes complaining that the Bank
of New Zealand, which declined to have its notes made
legal tender, has now stopped gold payments, and issues
Bank of N.S.W. paper instead. The reason is sufficiently
simple. For several days the Bank of N.Z. was the only
recognised gold-paying institution in Sydney. Therefore
people burdened with great quantities of “ legal tender ”
notes deposited them there on current account, and
blandly drew cheques which they demanded should be
paid in gold. The Act authorises the issue of £7,000,000
in paper, and as things were going the B.N.Z. would have
had to cash the lot and then wait indefinitely for its
money. Therefore, there not being void enough in the
province for such a contract, it began to pay local new
depositors who wanted gold in their own “ legal tender.”
• * •
Tribute follows boom at Hillgrove. The share-
holders’ money once absorbed in management, machinery
and wages, the miners, settled in the district, can do
nought but take what is left of the mines on tribute.
• * #
Mining by tribute-system is largely increasing
in N.S.W. With hard work, cheap crushing, and, above
all, most of the dear work done by defunct companies,
trihutors are averaging fair wages. About the best tribute
in the province is that of the New Bushman’s, Parkes,
held by ten men on a 10 per cent, royalty. Last crushing,
3620z5. from 168 tons. Reef about a foot and well
opened-up.
• * *
A large contractor paid into one of the recently-
suspended N.S.W. banks some thousands of pounds,
chiefly to be spent in wages, within a few hours of the
notice of suspension. It is only one of many cases
differing only in degree. But, can one instance be given
of a bank director, or manager, doing likewise?
# * *
Is this a conundrum ? How will the Australian
banking-collapse be set down in Australian history? Will
it be believed that the banks were the innocent victims of
stupid public panic, or that the public were victims of the
greatest bank-robbery of the century ?
# * *
Now that the depositors who got their money out
of the banks are cursed all round for their want of con-
fidence, it would be well (in the interests of public confi-
dence) if bank managers and directors would give some
proof of their own. It would be very interesting to know
the number and amount of cheques, if any, drawn by
directors and their confidential friends, say within a week
before putting up the shutters. To hold a bank-director-
ship at pre-reconstruction time must, indeed, he a trying
ordeal. You meet in th ■ street your grey-headed lifelong
friend, already apoplectic and shaky in the knees. The
loss of his fortune will be for him sentence of death. A
word from you, and he will withdraw in gold. But no;
perfectlv aware that even now the reconstruction scheme
is in the hands of the printer, you tell old Billy Hardcash,
who went to school witti you, to have confidence, and that
all is going swimmingly.
# ■ • m
Mr. Wilson, the Victorian dairy expert, has
visited Lord Hopefoun’s farms in Scotland, and says that
the latest Scotch milking-machine is a failure. The latest
Scotch milking-machines are the reconstructing banks.
They aren’t milking failures, anyhow.
# # #
In these “ pending-reconstruction ’’ days, the
memory of the confidence formerly displayed by company
officials is as the shadow of a great rock in a weary land.
One Melbourne co. had a genial manager and several
equally genial directors. One of these last dropped in
one day, and the manager asked him to sign a cheque.
“ Oh, all right, and while I’m at it I might as well sign
the lot.” he attached his sign-manual to a
couple of hunarea- blank cheques. The same afternoon
another director came along, and to him said the
manager,’ '* woft- nilght sign these cheques after Mr.
Smith; save a lot of trouble.” He hadn’t time lust then,
so took the book home with him, and returned it in the
morning fully signed up. This ought to have led to
something in the Mat. Larkin or G. N. Taylor line, but
it didn’t. • Unfortunately for the evening papers that
manager was an honest man.
# # *
The last balance-sheet of the National Mutual
Life Office shows that the accumulated funds now amount
to £1,259,573, and the annual income to about £290,000.
The claims paid during the year amounted to £85,000, of
which over £70,000 was covered by interest received from
investments. The National Mutual is second only to the
A.M.P. in the liberality of its bonuses, and is doing a
large and steadily increasing business.
* * *
In reference to a recent Bulletin paragraph,
we are informed that the Mutual Life Co. of Austra-
lasia has also abolished the objectionable “ suicide
clause ” in its policies, and, like tiie Colonial Mutual,
issues what may be fairly termed an ideal policy with no
conditions whatever as to residence, occupation, habits
of life, or cause of death. This is the example which
every insurance society ought to follow. It may take as
many precautions as it likes before accepting a risk, but
when once accepted it ought to be absolutely indefeasible.
Even if the assured falsely asserted that his father was not
alcoholic, and that his mother was not consumptive, and
concealed the fact that he himself had had a skull-frac-
ture and suffered from fits—still when once the office
takes the insurance it should take all chances—even if it
has to make a Small increase in premiums all round to
cover the risk. The old-fashioned restrictions are so
many loop-holes by which an institution may crawl out
of its liabilities, and in some offices they are so many and
so stringent that not more than 50 pet cent, of the policies
are absolutely safe and reliable;
# # *
At the meeting of shareholders in the Bank of
Victoria in Melbourne, Edward Miller; chairman of direc-
tors, tried in a mild way the Frank-Qrey-Snoith-feeling-a-
humiliated-man dodge, but it wouldn’t work. After all,
the Miller family comes out on top. It was popularly
supposed that they were as deeply in With the Victoria as
“ Big ” Clarke, junior, with the Colonial, and now it turns
out that the entire tribe of Millers have only some thou-
sand shares among thein.
• • •
When the Royal Bank of Queensland died last
week, hoping to rise again ,on the day of the reconstruction
of the dead, there was but a passing ripple of excitement.
The public Capacity for astonishment is about worn otit.
a • #
Once more in the Mercantile mess did the Age
(next day in a “ jesuitioal ” sub-leader) tamely follow the
Argus in damning O’Loghlen’s most damnable decision.
* * #
Advertising foi? sale by tender ah alleged cinna-
bar mine, at Bingara, N.S.W., the liquidator says : “ The
redeeming feature about these blocks is that they have
been unfavourably reported on by a Sydney expert.”
# # #
Even the mining men on the Randt are asking
particulars of the existence of diorite near to any paying
gold mine. A big mining man of American experience
asked the writer recently if he could tell him where gold
lived in diorite country ? These queries sound strange in
Australia. The many tons of gold turned out of Lucknow
were got between belts of diorite and serpentine rock.
Mitchell’s Creek freehold, one of the most consistent
mines of Australia, is in diorite ; Baker’s Creek itself is of
dioritio character ; Mt. M’Donald largely consists of
diorite—in fact, although there are not a great number of
good mines in N.S.W., most of them are in diorite.
* * *
What is believed to be the contact of the
Serpentine and diorite belt of country, from which many
tons of gold have been won at Lucknow, has been found
in one of Dalton’s paddocks at Orange, over six miles
from the Lucknow mines. Encouraging prospects of gold
have also been discovered.
* # ■ *
Two rushes have broken out within the last few
days—a tinfield in the vicinity of Condoblin, N.S.W., and
a goldfield at Burra Burra in the Parkes district.
Hundreds of men are at each field and hundreds on the
road there. Prospects on both fields appear to be
encouraging, but the writer has as yet no news on which
he can implicitly rely.
The Worker alleges that the commercial editor
Of a “ great ” Australian daily got down to his bank the
day before it burst, just five minutes too late to get out
his money. He had probably been detained writing a fiery
article on the folly and criminality of the people who in-
sisted on withdrawing their coin at the present juncture.
That was why he was late.
• # •
The fates have played it low down on the poet
FitzGihbon. That great literary authority went to Eng-
land, and raised a £1,000,000 loan on ruinous terms for
Melb. Board of Works. The amount was deposited in
equal shares in the Bank of Victoria, City of Melbourne
Bank, and Commercial of Australia, and they all closed
up and left the Board of Works to scramble round for an
overdraft.
» # •
A wealthy Hebrew, well known as a mining
speculator, was recently nominated for membership in the
Stock Exchange of Melbourne, and was blackballed. The
reason advanced for subjecting him to this indignity was
that he had only paid £3OO for the seat, whereas there is
an unwritten law fixing the minimum recognised value at
£lOOO.
# * *
Editor Bulletin, —Be your recent paragraph on
the Otis ore crusher. I have been working that mill for
six months. It will take quartz as big as half-brieks and
grind it to flour at the rate of J to 1J ton per hour,
according to mesh. We provide screens 30, 40, 50, 60,
and 70 per lineal inch, or 900, 1600, 2500, 3600, and 4990
per square inch. I would not take the gift of a stone-
breaker in connection with this mill, because the quartz-
hunks become boulders, and assist in the crushing as
well as the 120 steel balls, which weight 13 cwt. In
comparing the Otis ore crusher with a 20-head battery,
the following is fair: Output, about equal; first cost,
much less ; cost of carriage to mine, one-fourth ; cost of
erection at mine, one-fourth ; cost of driving, one-half ;
cost of fuel, one-half ; cost of maintenance, one-fourth ;
cost of attendance, one-fourth ; cost of gratings, one-
twentieth. Crushes wet or dry. No dust when dry
crushing. No waste of power in re-crushing ore already
crushed to required grade. Constant work as against
time and power lost while stampers are rising and falling.
Area of gratings 50 square feet, exposing 1250 square feet
per minute as against 12 square feet in a battery. The
Otis ore crusher is the coming mill for reducing quartz,
sandstones, slates, dykes, tailings, headings—all ores
containing gold, silver, copper, antimony, &0., either wet
or dry, and to any grade required, to the fineness of fine
fl our . Ay ours faithfully, Robert Brown, Otis Ore Crushing
Works, South Melbourne, May 6th, 1893.
* * #
The sequel to the action of the Orange miners
in carting their gold to Harry Newman’s house, and the
deposit of the coin pro tern, in Mrs. Newman’s name in
the Union Bank, is herewith told. Mrs. N. was found
camping on the door-mat of the Union on the Tuesday
morning, and upon the bank doors being opened, she
handed in documents and notes for four figures, request-
i.ng_£old, gold, gold ! This was just five minutes before
a wire was received that notes were proclaimed a legal
tender. Mrs. N. carted her cash out to Lucknow, armed
with a neat little six-chambered repeater, with which, it
is boasted, she can pink a fly at 12 paces. Upon Harry’s
telegraphic communication, later, the gold was taken
back to Orange and deposited as a trust account in the
suspended Commercial, where it now lies in the name of
Kitty Newman, whose ponies are reported, at latest
despatches, as being kept harnessed, while the repeating
popgun is in good going order.

A canny Scotch deacon of Pastor Bevan’s
Melbourne Church might lay claim to be a specially in-
gpired prophet. The spirit moved him not to bank th«
collections for four Sundays just prior to the bank
suspensions. What the inquisitive ungodly would like to
know is—Was the canny deacon related to a bank-
director?

* * *
What a horrid change has come o’er the spirit
of the re-construction dream all at orlce !
* # *
Manager Lane, of the Broken Hill Block 14
Co., alleges, in his latest half-yearly report, that, prior to
the strike, each workman in the mine raised and handled
but a little over 11 tons of ore per month, whereas, since
the introduction of the sloping by contract system, he
has been able to bring the figure to over 21 tons. lie
estimates this to represent a saving of 47.2 per cent. On
the working expenses of the mine.
* # *
The Junction Co. closed its last half-year
(during two months of which the mine was idle owing to
the strike) with a profit of £451 Bs. sd. The satisfaction
expressed at this result is based on the fact that the Co.
is treating at its Port Adelaide smelting works a fair per-
centage of its sulphides ; also, as there is but sixpence to
call upon the shares, the company has long been perilously
near to reconstruction.

Mr. J. W. Roberts explains that he has bought
a seat on the Victorian, hot on the Melbourne Stock
Exchange.
# • #
The very latest Melbourne commercial novelty
is a swell, canvassing for tomb-stones. His
funereal get up, high stepper and dog-cart are absolutely
irreproachable. The death-columns of the daily papers
give him the cue to the house of desolation, and the
grief being fresh and warm, the order for the stone is
put in hand even before the cadaver is planted. Se is
doing a roaring trade, merited by his cast-iron—enter-
prise,
• • *
Dibbs’ bank-crisis Act limits the circulatioh of
notes by the amount of a Dink's capital and reserve-fund.
To ensure the existence of the reserve-fund it should
provide that the amount be invested in Government
bonds and coin—otherwise the limitation is a hideohs
farce.
# * *
The dividend-paying mines of the Randt, taken
in the aggregate, have a market-value of 107 per cent,
greater than the capital stock issued, while the value of
hon-dividend-paying mines is 31 per cent, over their
capital. Comstock alone, in its best days, beat this
record.
* * fc
The tin lodes of N.S.W. are condemned as too
poor to pay, and many have been worked and abandoned
because the yield was not more than five per cent. A
man with a five per cent. lode tin-mine would be “fired
out "of most Sydney mining offices. Yet, the average of
Dalcoath, the great tin-mine o! the World, is only three
per cent, of block tin, and there are many paying tin-
mines in Cornwall whose return is from 50 to 701bs. bf tin
per ton of ore.
« * *
Copeland, called after the present N.S.W.
Lands Minister, had, 12 years ago, several magnificent
claims, of Which the Mountain Maid stood easily first.
The shoots were short, and so were their lives. Tb-day
Copeland enjoys the quietude of the tomb. How some
men live thete is an unfathomable mystery. But how
that men are settling down to hard steady work the
potentialities of Copeland will receive attention. Money
and common-sense management will probably again pick
up the old Mountain Maid shoots in the deeper grohnd.
The good reef left in the Hidden Treasure will again be
worked. The Centennial Tunrtel will one day be carried
to the reef and Copeland Will boom again.

they sold out loan English Co., which took £37,347135. 7d.
out of the hline from June '92 to Feb-. '93.
* « •
The Eureka, accounted one of the few regularly
payable mines in the Northern Territory, and which,
during 20 months ending 31st December last, turned out
£9447 worth of gold (£6OOO profit), was a few weeks ago
sold for £llOO to Tong King Chong, who had secured the
first refusal.
# # *
An Age mining “ expert," on the recent find at
Baldhead, near the Haunted Stream, Gippsland, says the
discoverers “struck a well-defined reef carrying splendid
gold, but whether it is in payable quantities has yet to be
proved.”
# * *
Not only do numerous Sydney solicitors nowa-
days engross their own deeds and registration copies, but
several firms, making large incomes, have made a stalk-
ing-horse of the hard times to discharge clerks wholesale,
under the pretence of necessary retrenchment. Then
these “ sweating ” lawyers get their articled clerks, who
pay large premiums—some as high as £3oo—and receive
only nominal salaries, to do the required engross-
ing gratis , while the discharged clerks, who are
generally married and familied men, join the unem-
ployed and live on whatever stored-up fat they
may chance to have. One Sydney lawyer, with a
large criminal practice, worth, say, £l5OO a year, runs his
office solely with articled clerks at a weekly cost of £2,
his “ managing clerk ” receiving the gorgeous stipend of
£1 per week. This noble employer, of his own motion,
allows his clerks two weeks’ holiday every Christmastide,
without pay. For absence through sickness pay is
stopped as per period. Another N.S.W. attorney, whose
practice brings in £3OOO a year, and who is a shining pillar
in so-called religious circles, employs as clerk a married
man with three children, paying him the heart-bleeding
sum of 30s. a week. And of such is the regiment of
Slieol.
« * *
According to Alfred Deakin, in his speech on
behalf of Director Bell, of the lamented Mercantile Bank,
that institution collapsed simply because of the “ stupid
absence of outside support.” Regarded as a sample of
barristerial gall, this utterance takes a high place.
# • *
Amalgam robberies from Randt batteries are so
numerous that a law is proposed enabling houses to be
searched and letters opened by the police. On the other
hand, the miners threaten to take the law into their own
hands should their households and correspondence be
outraged.
# * #
A second boom, local authorities say, is setting
in on the Randt, the enormous gold output of which is
attracting the world.
# # #
Money Wigrams insolvent! Alas for the glorious
days of the Lightnings and Marco Polos, with their rivals,
the White Stars and James Baineses !
# * •
Under the pressure of the cyanide competition
the cost of barrel chlorination is gradually being reduced
in America. The great problem remains— i-e., a cheaper
method of producing chlorine.
# # •
A local paper says there is a want of tone and
animation in the Bendigo share-market, except in a few
“gambling stocks,” and adds that “some new discoveries
would infuse life into it.” But not a word to indicate
what are the gambling stocks. Even in Bendigo the
truth, that yields and dividends are vapidly falling, is
glossed overj while increases are blazoned forth with
triurqph-tongued paragraphs.
# * *
Mr. J. A. Allan, for nine years manager of the
great Tharsis copper-mine in Spain, last week reported to
the Mount Lyell directors that he confirmed, without
hesitation, all Dr. Peters had previously told them about
the mine. Mr. Allan says that Mount Lyell mine can be
made to pay from now for an expenditure of £27,000.
There is now much speculation in shares.

“Stragglers”: A Sketch Out Back.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
BmAIL, oblong hub* Walled with blue-grey hartl-
Wood slabs, adzed at the ends and set horizontally
between the round sapling ” Studs } high toot of
the eternal galvanised iron. A big rubbish-heap
lies about a yard to the right of the door, which
Opens ftoni the middle of One of the side Walls ; it
blight be the front ot the back Wall — thebe’s no-
thiiig to fix it. Two tows of rOugh blinks, Of
berths; tun found thteb sides of the ititefcibr ; and
$ fireplace occupies one end -the kitchen end.
Sleeping, eating, gambling, and cooking accom-
modation for 30 men ini about 18 x 40 feet.
■The roiiseabouts and shearers use the hut in
common during shearing. Down the centre of the
place runs a table made of stakes driven into the
ground, with cross-pieces supporting a top of half
round slabs set with the flat sides up,: and affording
a few level places for soup-plates ; on each side
are-crooked, unbarked poles la'd in short forks, to
serve as seats. Item—the poles are worn smoothest
opposite the level places on the table. The floor
is littered with rubbish —old wool-bales, news-
papers, boots, worn-out shearing pants, rough
bedding, &0., raked out of thebunks in impatient
search for missing articles, signs of a glad and
eager departure, with cheques, when the shed last
ettt out. ,
To the west is a dam, holding back a broad,
shallow sheet of grey water, with dead trees
Standiiig irt it. . , ,
i,,Fih-thet hp aldiig this Water Is ,a brush sheaf-
ing : shed, i. a rough sframework of poles with a
brush roof. This kind, of shed has the adr
vantage of being cooler than iron. It is not
rain-proof, but shearers don’t' work in rainy
weather; shearing even slightly damp sheep is
considered the surest and quickest way to get the
worst kind of rheumatism. The floor is covered
with rubbish from the roof, and here and there
lies a rusty pair of shears. A couple of dry, tar-
pots hang by nails in the posts. , The “ board is
very uneven and must be bad for “sweeping up.
The pens are formed by round, crooked stakes
driven into the ground in irregular lines ; and the
whole business reminds one of the “ cubby-house
style of architecture of one’s childhood.
Opposite stands the wool-shed, built entirely or
galvanised-iron ; a blinding object to start out of
the scrub on a burning hot day. God forgive the
man who invented galvanised-iron, and the giecd
which introduced it into Australia; for you
couldn’t get worse roofing material for a hot
country. , ... , ,
The wool-washing, soap-boiling, and wool-press-
ing arrangements are further up the dam. Go-
vernment House ” is a nqle away, and is nothing
better,than a bush hut,, for this station belongs to
ac company. And .the eompanybelongs to a bank.
And the bank belongs to England mostly.
Mulga scrub all round,,and, in between, patches
of reddish sand where the grass ought to be.
It is New Year’s Eve. Half-a-dozen “ travel-
lers ” are camping in the hut, having a spell.
They want it, for there are 20 miles of dry lignum
plain between here and the Government bore to
the East; and about 18 miles of heavy, sandy
“cleared road ” North West to the next water in
that direction. With one exception, the men do
not seem “ hard-up ; ” at least, not as that condi-
tion is understood by the swagmen of these times.
The least lucky one of the lot had three weeks
work in a shed last season, and there might pro-
bably be five iponnds amongst the whole crowd.
They are all shearers, or, at least, they say they
are. Some might be “only rousers.”
These men have a kind of stock hopeof getting
a few stragglers to shear somewhere; but their
main object is to live till next shearing. In order
to do this they must tramp for tucker, and trust
to the regulation—and partly mythical—pint o
flour, and bit o’ meat, or tea and sugar, and to the
goodness of cooks and storekeepers and bound ry-
riders. You cart only depend on getting tucker
once at One jilace j then you must tramp on to the
fiext; If han’t get it once yort must go short,
but there’s a lot df fenergjf, in art etajJtjjr stomach.
If you get an extra supply, you may camp for a
day and have a spell. To live you must walk.
To stop walking, is to die. ,
The Exception is an outcast amongst bush out-
casts, and looks better fitted for Sydney Domain.
He lies on the bottom of a galvanised iron
ease, with a piece of blue-blanket, for a pillow.
He is dressed in a blue cotton jumper, a,
pair of very old and ragged tweed trousers,
and one boot and one slipper. He found the
slipper in the last', shed, and the boot in the rub-
bish-heap here. When his own boots gave out he
walked 150 miles with his feet roughly sewn up in
pieces of sacking from an old. wool-bale. No sign
of a patch, or an attempt at mending; anywhere
about his clothes, and that’s a bad sign; for, when
a swagraan leaves off mending or patching his
garments, his case is about hopeless. The Excep-
tion’s swag consists of the aforesaid piece of blanket
rolled up and tied with pieces of rag. He has no
tvatet bag j carries his Water in a billy ; and how
fie manages without ft bag is a mystery known
only to himself. Hp has read every scrap bf P r ' r }* ;
within reach, and now lies on, his side, with Ins
face to the wall and one arm thrown up oyer his
head ; the jumper is twisted back, and leaves his
skin bare from hip to arm-pit. His, lower
face is brutal, his eyes small and shifty, and ugly
straight lines run across his low forehead. He
says very little, but scowls most of the time—poor
devil. lie might be, or, at least seem, a totally
different man under more favourable conditions.
Heds probably a—well, a free labourer.
A very sick jackeroo lies in one of the bunks*
A sandy, sawney-looking Bonrke native takes
great interest in this wreck; watches bis every
movement as though he never saw a sick man
before. The men lie about in the bunks, or trte
shade of the hut, and rest, and read all the soiled
and mutilated scraps of literature they can rake
out of the rubbish, and sleep, and wake up
swimming in perspiration, and growl about the
heat. , ~, j
It is hot, and two shearers cats —a black and a
white one- sit in one 'of the upper bunks with
their little red tongues out, panting like dogs.
These cats live, well during shearing, and take
their chances tlfe test of the year—same as shed
rouseabouts have to do. , They seem glad tp see
the “ traveller come ; lie makes things mors
homelike. They curl and sidle affectionately round
the table-logs, and the legs of tbs men, and purr,
and carry their masts up, and regard the cooking
with feline interest and approval, and look as
cheerful as cats can—and contented. God knows
how many tired, dusty, and sockless ankles they
rub against in their time.
Now and then a man takes his tucker-bags and
goes down to the station for a bit of flour, or meat,
or tea, or sugat, choosing the time when the
manager is likely to bp out on the run. Tfm cook
here is a li good cook*” from £t traveller’s point of
view ; too good to keep Iris placp long, ( , , ,
Occasionally someone gets soipo water in an old
kerosene-tin, and washes a shirt or pair of trousers,
and a pair or .two of socles —or foot-rags, .1 hat is,
he soaks some of the stiffness opt of these articles.
Three times a day the black billies and cloudy
nose-bags are placed on the table. ihe men eat
in a casual kind of way, as though it were only a
Custom of theirs, a matter of form—a habit which
could be left off if it were worth while.
The Exception is heard to Remark to no one m
particular, that he’ll give all he has for a square
meal. , , ,
“An’ ye’d get it cheap, bigod! says a lug
Irish shearer. “ Come and have dinner with us ;
there’s plenty there.” .
,fiut the Exception only eats a few roouthfalls,
afid his appetite is gone | his stomach has become
contracted, perhaps. .1 j .
~ The Wreck cannot eat at All, and , seems inter-
nally disturbed by the sight of others eating. ,
One of the men is a cook, and this morning he
volunteered good-naturedly to bake bread for the
rest. His mates amuse themselves by check-
ing” him. , .
“ I’ve heard he’s a dirty and slow cook, says
one. ■ ‘ ' '
. “Ah ! ” says the cook, “you’ll bp, glad to come
to me for a pint of flour when I’m cooking and
you’re on the track, some day.’’
Sunset. Some of the men sit at the end of the
hut to get the fulhbenefit of a breeze which comes
from the West. A great bank of “ rain clouds
is rising in that direction, but no one says he
thinks it’ll rain; neither does anybody think
we’re going to have, some rain. None_ but the
greenest jackeroo would venture that risky and
foolish observation. Out here, it can look more
like rain without raining, and continue to do so
for aLlonger time than in mpst other places,
Tfle Wreck went down to,the station this after-
noon to get some medicine and bush medical
advice. The Bourke savvney helped him to do up
his swag; he did it with an awed look and
as though lie thought it a great distinction to be
allowed to touch the belongings of such a curiosity.
It was afterwards generally agreed that it was a
good idea for the Wreck to go to the station ; he d
get some physic and a bit o’ tucker to take him on.,
“For they’ll give tucker to a sick man sooner than
to a chap what’s all right.” , . j.
The Exception is rooting about in the rubbish
for the other blucher boot. ~
The men get a little more sociable, and feel
each other to find out who’s “ union,” and talk
about water, and exchange hints as to good
“ tucker-tracks,” and discuss the strike, and curse
the squatter (which is all they have got to curse),
and growl about union leaders, and tell lies against
each other sociably. There are tally lies ; and lies
about getting tucker by trickery ; and long-tramp-
with-heavy-pwag and-no-water lies; and lies about
getting the jb'esfc of sqiiatters and bosses-over-the-
board ; ; and droving, fighting, racing, gambling
and drinking lies. Lips ad and every
true Australian bushman must try his best to tell
a bigger out-back lie than the last bush-liar.
Pat is not quite easy in his mind. He found an
old pair of pants in the scrub this morning, and
can’t decide whether they are better than his own,
or, rather, whether his own are worse —if that’s
possible. He doesn’t want to increase the weight
of his swag unnecessarily by taking both pairs.
He reckons that the pants were thrown away when
the shed cut-out last, but then they might have
been lying out exposed to the weather for a longer
period. *Tls rather an important question, for it’s
vert annoying, after you’ve mended and patched
tin old pa it Of pants, to find, when a day or two
further on the track, that they ate worse than the
ones you left behind. y
There is some growling about the water here,
and one of the men makes a billy Of tea, Ihe
water is better cooked. Pint-pots and _sngar-bagg
are groped out and brought to the kitchen hut,
and each man fills his pannikin, the Irishman
keeping a thumb on the edge of his, so as to knoW
when the pot’s full, for it’s very dark and there’s
no more firewood. You, soon know way,,
especially if you’re in the habit of pressing lighted
tobacco down into your pipe with the top of your
thumb. The old slush-lamps are all burnt out.
Each man feels for the mouth of his sugar-bag
with one hand while he keeps the bearings of his
pot with the other.
The Irishman has lost his match-box, and feels
for it all over the table without success. He
stotips dOwtt With his hands on his knees, gets the
table-top on a level With the flicker of firelight,
and “moons ” the object, sts it Were.
Time to turn ip. It is very dark inside kiit

, Some of the men will roll up their sw,ags on the

HENRY LAWSON.
The Land of Light.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
If I could fly, on wing so fleet,
Beyond the pulse of passing time,
And future moments strangely greet
While fret the world',, with step sublime,
Pressed on mfr track—behind, so far—
No pause I’d make in the swift flight
Thro’ vaster space to phantom star
Till I beheld the Land of Light.
For who cquld bear the soulless day
Which slowly waits the doom of man,'
And binds him down to dull dqcay —
The sentence since the world began—
If magic wing were given him
To break the chain which bound him tight
To present time, that he might swim
Far out unto the Land of Light ?
The wider view—for which we yearn,
That we might know the things to come,
And, by far flight, the truth discern—
Be mine, to seek the mighty sum
Of human care and equal hope,
The light of day, the dark of night,
And all that rests within the scope <
Of that fair sphefe—the Land of Light.
In spirit, then, let fancy dart ,■,, ,
From earthly .bound to farthest thought,
And there survey,i with lighter heart,
The perfect home the soul had sought.
Who rests content with ebb and flow
Of passing time knows no delight;
For him the mind shuts out its glow —
The vision bright—the Land of Light.
Sydney.
